The 77th Section.
In cutting off limbs and breaking bones.
The limbs may be affected either from an external cause or an internal one, so if you treated that decay with medicinal treatment and the treatment did not succeed and saw the decay spreading in the member without anything stopping it.
In that case, it is necessary to cut off that member up to where the decay has reached in order for the patient to be saved from death or great suffering, which is greater than the loss of a member. And an indication of this is when the member becomes blackened until you think the fire has burned it.
When the corruption spreads beyond the wound until it reaches another part of the body and takes hold in a general manner, then begin to cut; similarly, if the cause of corruption is due to some kind of vermin such as the sea scorpion or the snake or the lizard, etc.
If the corruption or gangrene is in the tip of the finger, then cut off the finger and do not let the corruption spread to the hand; similarly, if it has taken hold in the hand, then cut it off at the wrist and do not let the corruption spread until it takes hold in the arm.
Similarly, if it has taken hold in the arm, then cut off the arm at the shoulder joint; if the corruption spreads and reaches the neck, do not cut off the neck at all, for that would be fatal to the patient, and instead apply another treatment according to what is possible.
And similarly, do the same with the leg; if the corruption takes hold in the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it has taken hold in the thigh, cut off the entire leg; if it spreads to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, then there is no remedy except to leave it and let the patient succumb to death; as for cutting off the limb or dividing it, tie a strap at the bottom of the place you want to cut and another strap above it.
And have one servant stretch the single strap down, and have another servant stretch the upper strap up, and strip away the flesh between the straps with a broad knife until all the flesh is exposed; then cut or divide. It should be noted from all sides that the linen cloth should be torn.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and expose the patient to unnecessary pain and hot swelling; if bleeding occurs during your work, stop the bleeding quickly or apply some stopping agents for blood then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then bind the injured member with a binding that is suitable for it and treat it until it heals. And I inform you of an example that was presented to a man on his leg, this very same one which I have described to you. That is, a bruise appeared in his leg with burning like fire.
And that corruption was the first thing to occur on his finger until the man took it all off, and the man cut himself from it when he saw the corruption spreading in the member with the severity of the pain and burning, and then he recovered.
So when a long time had passed, the same corruption appeared on his thumb and he came to me. I applied a dressing to that part with the medications I had brought for his hand after cleaning his body, but the corruption did not stop and continued spreading in another finger.
Until the corruption spread to his hand and I was asked to cut off his hand. I refused, hoping that the dressing would bring relief to that part and fearing also for him if his hand were cut off because the man's strength was on the point of failing. And when I gave up hope he went away to his town.
And it was reported to me that he cut off his own hand and recovered. I narrated this story in order for it to be a warning against what happens in cases like this disease, and as an example to be followed and acted upon.
The Eighth and Eighty-Ninth Chapter.
In the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicines.
If a tumor occurs in some of the fleshy members and it lasts for a long time until it reaches its term, then it bursts or boils and all that was inside comes out, leaving the place empty as if it were an empty vessel.
And the skin on it is like a patch that has peeled off and was not severe in its corruption so as to affect bone or nerve or ligament. And for this reason, it deserves to be called an abscess; it should not be called a boil unless it prolongs its term until the corruption affects something from these members.
When that is the case, it is called nassura or zukama. Its treatment is to cut off all of that skin as it devolves and especially if it has become flabby and has become like a piece of cloth and you are certain that it does not adhere to the place for its corruption.
If the skin does not adhere and it has not reached that degree of corruption, and there is a thick layer of fat under it, then use the syringe. It is to look at the swelling; if it is large and the pus that flows from it has a foul smell, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
What is it that is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and ginger, each part equal, gathered together in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and becomes like the thickness of honey. Then take what you need from it and mix it with water and honey, and inject it into the swelling, and tie up his mouth and leave the medicine there for two hours.
Then remove it with the scraper. Do this for a few days until the swelling is cleansed and the stench goes away, or if this medicine is not available, then inject it with ashes of grape wood or ash of oak wood, by taking the ash and adding water to it.
Then wash it off and inject it with that until you are certain that the swelling has been cleansed. If there is no hope in this case, then use the Egyptian medicine or ashes of grape wood and oak wood, but if not, then inject it with water and honey to which a little crushed ginger has been added.
Or inject it with honey and wine mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and the wine adheres to the swelling and especially if the wine contains a good binding agent and coagulant. If you do this several times and are certain that the swelling's corruption has gone away, then inject it with what promotes flesh growth in it.
Like taking the ointment and mixing it with glue, wine and astringent liquid or binding it with some medicines and other ointments that we have mentioned in the article on ointments; if the mouth of the syringe is narrow, it does not fit.
Expand it with a little iron or put in a malleable piece of papyrus, Egyptian ointment or syrian ointment until it fits; and similarly if the mouth is also wide, join its lips together with sutures and leave enough for the syringe to enter without excess.
And similarly if the mouth which discharges pus is elevated upwards, split it in its lowest part so that the pus may flow downwards because if the pus accumulates in the bottom of the syringe it will prevent the flesh from growing inside.
If you cannot split the syringe downwards as you want, make it tilt so that the pus may flow out easily according to what you prefer and ensure that nothing of the pus remains inside at all.
And as for the medicated ointments that are placed on the syringe from outside and how to place them on it, it is that you take one of the medicated ointments similar to what you want and spread it on a linen cloth so that the cloth be long enough to cover the entire syringe. Then cut a slit with scissors opposite the mouth of the syringe, slightly wider than the mouth of the syringe; then spread one of the soft ointments on another cloth to the size of the slit and place it on it in a way that is not opposite the mouth of the syringe so as to make the pus come out easily; and do not remove the large cloth except afterwards.
Several days, and as for that on the mouth of the syringe, it is what you remove every time to make what has accumulated there come out and to know whether what flows from it is much or little or putrid or not.
And with this, inspect the position of the wound itself: whether its owner feels pain there or not; whether it is inflamed or not; whether there is an abscess or not. If you treat it thus and see that the matter that emerges is slight at the mouth of the wound.
Know that the wound has become stuck or almost stuck; then release it on the third day at the latest. When you have dressed the wound, replace the dressing with another and reapply the poultice. If a thin pus emerges from the wound after a long period, do not despair of its closure.
So heal quickly, for some people's flesh does not form well in their wound, and others are contrary to that. If pus emerges from the wound after many days which is not clotted, know that the wound has not healed; if the healing of the wound is slow.
And its extent is long; increase in drying out medicines, and let the medicines be wet in their essence and dry in their force, like the poultice of the white sap if an addition has been made to it. And what cures better with it is that you take the poultice of the white sap.
And it is resolved with a mixture of wax and rose water, then sprinkle the old drink over it, in its essence. Then mix it with it and use it. And you can do this if the poultice of the white sap is not available, by mixing honey well until it becomes foamy and used or taking from the gallnut, patience, and khindar, crushing all together and sprinkling it over the honey while it is on fire. Then spread it on a cloth and apply it to the wound or spread honey that you have mixed well until it thickens, and sprinkle medicines over it, and place the cloth on it and bind it.
And it may be used in the same way with the long ziraun, the root of the Asmanjuni sasoun, and the fine grains of kersennah, or a single, or multiple qantariyun, crushing them and sieving them, and sprinkling them over the honey that you described, and using it. If the wound's condition continues to deteriorate without being healed by this treatment, know that there is decay in its depth.
The treatment of the Naser has a profound effect on the bones, or on any of the hard arteries or ligaments that surround it.
The 88th chapter.
In the treatment of the diseased tongue and gums, amputation of the excess finger and division of the fingers' articulation.
The tongue is a thick mucous that grows under the nail of the thumb or foot, and may grow on other fingers; if it persists and its treatment is neglected, it will become swollen and hot with inflammation.
Or it will putrefy and become foul until it eats away at the base of the nail, or even all of it, and may spread to the bone so that it has a bad smell and the tip of the finger becomes flat and takes on a bluish color; if you treat it as described in the division and your treatment does not succeed.
It is necessary to cut away with iron all the excrescence that grows from the nail, then to cauterize the wound afterwards; for cauterization is very beneficial in this case. As for if the bone is sound and the nail also is sound.
And if the corner of the nail has pushed the flesh inward and caused it to ulcerate and suppurate, then you should put a thin probe underneath the corner of the nail that pushes the flesh and lift it up and cut away that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh burning and caustic remedies until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. As for if the putrefaction has affected the bone, then you should cut away that bone and remove it; for it will not heal as long as there is rotten bone present.
If you see the inflammation and putrefaction spreading in the finger, then cut it off at one of the joints as described earlier, and treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or bruised and there is severe pain, then apply a poultice first.
Then cut the nail with a sharp, angled incision from above to below and be careful not to reach the flesh underneath the nail, for this would cause the patient great pain and lead to an excess of flesh in that place. Then treat the area as necessary to alleviate the pain.
And as for the extra fingers that occur in some people's hands, they may be entirely fleshy, or in some cases may contain bones, or even nails and tendons. And sometimes it can grow from the joint of some fingers.
Or it may grow from some joints of the fingers, and those that grow from some joints do not move, while those that grow at the joint of the finger may move. If what grows is only fleshy, it can be cut off easily, by cutting it at its origin with a broad knife.
And as for what grows at the origin of the joint, its treatment is difficult and it is better not to cut it off. But if what grows is on a finger at one of its joints, you should first cut away the flesh in curved cuts up to the bone, then spread the bone with one of those suitable instruments.
Then treat it until it is healed. And as for the fusion that occurs between some fingers, many times this happens and it is a natural occurrence in humans and can occur when an injury or burn and the like occurs, so you should cut through that fusion.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a splint or an opening made of vermilion ointment so that it does not fuse quickly and separate them, or make between them a thin lead plate until the area heals as it should.
And similarly, if fusion occurs between some fingers of the hand, cut through that fusion according to what is needed and suitable for the shape of the limb.
The Ninetieth Chapter.
On Cutting Veins.
The veins are twisted, hard vessels filled with black blood that occur most frequently in the body's members and particularly in the legs, especially in the feet, calves, and hamstrings; therefore, one should first use gentle massage on the body several times.
Then ligate the patient's pulse, and as for treating it with iron, it will be in two ways: one is to cut and extract the black blood, and the other is to remove the sweat and extract it entirely; but cutting is done in this manner: first warm the leg with hot water.

The ninetieth chapter.
In the incision of the middle flesh.
This flesh forms in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and Arab lands, and in regions with little arable land; it may also form in other parts of the body besides the legs and is caused by a swelling that occurs under the skin.
As happens in the bodies of living creatures, and boils and carbuncles that develop between the skin and flesh, and the sign of the beginning of this flesh is that it begins to boil severely in the leg, then the place becomes inflamed, and then the flesh emerges from that inflammation.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, so when one of its parts appears, it is necessary to wrap it around a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, tie it and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it is very long, cut off some of it and wrap the rest without cutting it from its root before everything has emerged, for if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, inflammation, and a bad abscess.
Therefore, it should be pulled and drawn out slowly little by little until everything has emerged from its last part, without leaving anything inside the body. Sometimes, this flesh in some people may emerge to a length of five ells and ten.
I have been told that a man has left me with twenty spans, so if he dies on you while you are treating him then insert a rod into his wound and extend it far into the body until everything in it is evacuated and try to clean the area with medicine for days.

The second ninetieth chapter.
In the cut on the vermiform appendix that arises under the caecum and is known as the cause of cattle.
This disease is known in some of our countries as the cause of cattle, because it often occurs in cattle. It is a small worm that arises between the skin and flesh and spreads throughout the body, rising and falling.
It becomes manifest with itching as it moves from one member to another until it bursts, and where it has burst in the skin, a swelling forms and it emerges, and its contents are like purulence of some of the excretions, as is seen in worms, vermin, and pumpkin in the abdomen.
And it is expected from its severity that if it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it up and emerge, and destroy the eye, which often occurs. If you want to treat it and remove it, then this can only be done when it moves and becomes apparent with itching.
You should tie what is above it and below it with a strong band, then cut through it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, carry out the procedure on the affected area with fire until it burns it, and the most severe damage that can be expected is to the eye as we mentioned.
If you see it has become near the eye in the head, then bind it under the forehead tightly and cut it out and remove it. The patient should be advised to purify his body with soothing medicines for foul humors and beware of nourishments that foster corruption.
The Third Ninetieth Chapter.
In the cut on the patient known as the Nafir.
This disease which is known to us as Nafir, is a pain that appears in some organs and then transfers from one organ to another, and I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was summoned to a woman patient in some rural areas and I uncovered her arm, and she showed me a slight swelling in the armpit of her arm. When I stayed with her for an hour, I saw that swelling moving up like a worm as it ascends to her shoulder as fast as can be, like pitch.
If it moves from place to place, the pain will depart from that spot and settle in the shoulder, then I sat for an hour and it spread throughout her body until it reached the other arm, then she told me that it is moving all over her body as I witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one limb to another and I had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a form before, except that I saw some people who found pain moving from one limb to another, but I did not see it as I saw it in this woman.
And I did not attribute that except to the fact that the woman was a Bedouin, her body rough and exposed, with visible veins, so it became apparent to the senses that this moving humors and it was necessary that it would not appear in the same way among the people of refinement and those with smooth bodies and hidden veins.
So if you want to treat it and the patient feels the pain, then as I described, it will appear before your eyes. Bind him above and below with a bandage and cut into him until that congested humors is released and the place is cleared.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by rubbing the body and what drives away the wind and stirs it up like a mustard plaster and a poultice and similar remedies.
The Ninetieth Chapter.
In Extracting Arrows.
The arrows may differ according to their kinds and the parts of the body where they strike, as well as their differences in kind. Some are large and small, some are glass-coated and smooth, some have three angles and four angles.
And some have fragments, and as for those that occur according to the organs where they strike, then there are two kinds: either the organs are main organs coated with a membrane like the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestine, bladder, and similar ones. So when an arrow strikes one of these organs and symptoms of death appear as I have described to you, then it is necessary to avoid extracting that arrow from there, for death will befall its owner in most cases. And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not lodged in the depths of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And as for the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the peritoneum on the brain, then it will be exposed to severe headache, fever, and dizziness.
And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not lodged in the depths of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And as for the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the peritoneum on the brain, then it will be exposed to severe headache, fever, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, trembling, and confusion of mind, followed by a fit or convulsion. Sometimes blood may flow from the nostrils or ears, sometimes speech may be lost and the voice gone, and liquid may issue from the wound like white matter resembling flesh.
So if these signs appear to you, stop treating the patient and do not extract the arrow if it has not already been removed. As for the symptoms of the arrow, if it strikes the heart and is close to the left breast and you feel as though it has lodged in something hard rather than something hollow.
As it may happen that the arrow moves like a pulse and flows from the wound black blood, and subsequently the limbs become chilled and cold and pale; therefore know that death is imminent and unavoidable. The sign of the arrow if it has pierced the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels near the neck swell, and the patient's complexion changes, and he breathes rapidly high up and asks for cold air; if the arrow has reached the diaphragm that is in the chest then it will be near the small ribs and there will be great breathing with severe pain.
And the patient becomes rigid and all the limbs on both sides are affected, if the arrow has reached the liver then it will be followed by severe pain and black blood similar to the liver in its color will come out of the wound; if the arrow has reached the intestines perhaps something that is not digested will come out of the wound.
The case is clear, if the arrow has reached the abdomen and is stuck there and something from feces or urine or excrement comes out of the wound or intestine which has burst then there is no hope for its treatment nor for removing the arrow; if the arrow has reached the bladder and urine has come out.
And something comes out from it to the outside and the pain intensifies on the patient, so know that he is dead; as for other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, back, shin and similar organs they have submitted to the greatest decree.
If the arrow did not hit a major blood vessel or bone and the arrow was not poisoned, I will tell you some of what I have seen happen with these arrows so that you can infer from it how to treat yours; this is because an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
So I extracted it from him on the other side under the earlobe and he recovered without any harm to his eye, and I extracted another arrow that had struck a Jew who had been hit in the corner of his eye beneath the lower eyelid and the arrow was embedded and only the small end that is attached to the shaft remained.

So when thirty days or so had passed and nothing changed with him, I split the arrow and probed it on him and took it out and he recovered. And no evil event occurred to him. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, so the wound became festering on him.
So when seven years had passed, the arrow came out at its root. And I saw a woman struck by an arrow in her belly, and the wound became festering and the arrow remained, and nothing changed with her nor was she finding any harm from anything of her natural actions.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound became festering and remained without him feeling much pain. And there are many like this. Then the surgeon sits on a pillow that is higher than the one he sits on, then he extends his arm and massages the patient with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the bandage together with the assistant, doing it two or three times, gently.
Because the tightening should be moderate, either excessive tightening would prevent blood flow or too little tightening would also prevent it; then after tightening, the assistant should massage his hands all together with a bunch.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the surgeon should gently clean the area around the incision with a small amount of old oil, especially; then he should place his ring finger on the left hand on the same vein under the site where he wants to make the incision slightly so that the vein does not escape from being struck. Because there are veins that flee from the incision like a thread, and some are filled with air; when the surgeon places his finger on them, they go down under the incision and deceive him, so that the incision does not open up the vein, and if it opens, then it only opens slightly.
So the surgeon should be careful and cautious in all these matters, then he should go down on his finger; if the vein opens up at that spot, then otherwise he should repeat it again once more under or above that spot with a gentle pressure if the area does not become inflamed.
If the wound becomes inflamed or swells, he should leave it for a day or two and not tighten the bandage; perhaps he may bring forth a warm boil and will not enter the bath, then he can repeat the incision if desired; if the puncture was small and the bleeding was slight.
Be careful not to let the blood flow more than you want. Have the operator in the same opening, align it with the straightness and open it a little more. Do that with the drill before the place becomes inflamed. Many people's places become inflamed when opened slightly.
If you see it becoming inflamed, do not touch it at all, because it will not benefit you. Instead, put something from oil on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this place than oil itself or other unguents, likewise.
Use the oil's residue in all your bloodletting of veins when there is difficulty in letting out the blood. And sometimes, the theriac of Farouk and the Shakhzaniya can be used if one of them is applied to the place, for the blood will flow and become thin if it is thick.
If a lump occurs in the place of bloodletting and it is large, this often happens especially to those who have not bled except that one time or if the vein has been opened slightly. Then hurry, place a damp sponge soaked in hot water and salt on the lump, squeeze it for an hour, and it will dissolve.
And it is necessary to do this after the blood has come out of the vein itself, either completely or from another vein. If something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after a few days, that does not harm. If you wish, then apply something of vinegar and honey mixed together.
Or something of vetch's juice and the like. And many times a lump and swelling occur when bloodletting is done from the saphenous veins. Place your hand on it, if you find it tender when you press on it, then that is a bad swelling, so beware not to apply anything we mentioned earlier.
For it is possible that blood may flow from the artery, but stop it with what is necessary to constrict it and make the place firm. Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. And it is necessary to let out of each person as much blood as his strength allows, according to the prevailing color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it be drained until it turns red. Similarly, if it is thick, let it be drained until it thins out. Similarly, if it is sharp, let it be drained until its sharpness subsides. And it is necessary for one who is full and needs to drain blood to do so all at once.
That he should dilate the vein and be broad, and whoever is weak is opposite to that. It is necessary for him to remove it gradually and the puncture to be narrow. And the best thing to use in draining the pus is one that opens up a gap without being pierced.
And this type of incision is free from bleeding and cutting nerves, and it is the best and most safe method of drainage. And whoever is accustomed to using deceit when draining should be given something to eat beforehand, such as bread soaked in almond or rosewater milk.
If he is tranquil and the blood is drained from him in three or four times, then if his temperament is relaxed, let him take bread soaked in rosewater or honey mixed with wine before the incision.
If deceit occurs during the incision and it is due to excessive blood flow, then let him be given water of meat and refined rosewater drink. He should use expensive perfume and anoint his chest with it, and use everything we mentioned in the chapter on deceit.
Whoever needs to empty his arm and release his blood again. And if someone's incision is for purging and his strength is weak, let him release the blood little by little as much as possible in consecutive days.
And if someone wants to relax his arm and release his blood again, and his body is strong, let him do so after the first incision on the seventh or ninth hour. And if he wants to draw the blood from his body towards the opposite side it has bent towards, then let him do so on the second or third day.
And as for one who has excess blood in his body, it has boiled and become hot and caused fever, then it is necessary to let out the blood all at once and to remove a great deal of it, and to widen the opening until the membrane is exposed after all the conditions of venesection have been fulfilled. And place your hand on his pulse while letting out the blood so that death does not occur instead of the membrane; for it often happens like that if the one venesectioning is unaware and neglectful. And do not intend to release the arm again and let out more blood.
And if the armpit has closed up and it is difficult to let out the blood, then strike him forcefully or twist with great strength; for that is very beneficial. Either leave it until it opens up again, or use a lancet to cut open what has clotted in the armpit.
Or put something of salt, which is dissolved in water, or some Farouq's powder or Shikhzaniya on him and press it gently until the blood comes out. If the armpit has swollen, leave it as it is.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity requires letting out blood again, then either open it above that spot or open another arm or armpit.
And as for bleeding the arm vein, it is replaced by letting out blood from the lacrimal gland and palatine vein if they are not found or are hidden because it is composed of them. And the manner of doing so is that the one performing venesection should put his hand in hot water until the cord becomes red and the armpit becomes clearly visible, then tie it above with a bandage somewhat tightly but moderately.
Then make the incision on the armpit slightly obliquely, neither too wide nor too long. And let the opening be wide and make your incision above the wrist joint slightly, if the blood does not come out, then put the arm back in the hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you reach your need, if you are in the summer days, you can dispense with washing the hand in hot water and even more so that it makes blood flow in hot water during winter, and this sweating is safer from all veins. Because there is no vein or nerve underneath, and as for the phlebotomy of the right hand, it is beneficial for the liver's ailments; how to do it: grasp the wrist of the hand with a bandage or your own hand after putting it in hot water until the sweat swells.
Because there is no vein or nerve underneath, and as for the phlebotomy of the right hand, it is beneficial for the liver's ailments; how to do it: grasp the wrist of the hand with a bandage or your own hand after putting it in hot water until the sweat swells. It becomes clear to the patient quickly, then you release it slightly and if you cut off all of it, that will not harm him; be careful not to insert your hand into the incision because underneath is a nerve of the fingers and the place is exempt from flesh, then you return the hand to hot water.
And let the blood flow in it because if you do not put it in hot water, the blood will congeal in the vein and refuse to flow; when you remove as much of the needed blood as possible, apply oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And likewise, you should do this for every narrow vein; and as for the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and likewise do this in phlebotomy as you did in the second one, whether right or left; and as for phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefits are for diseases below the body such as uterus ailments.
And persistent hemorrhoids and kidney ailments, and ulcers of the thighs and legs, chronic diseases and others; how to do it: his leg enters hot water and a poultice is placed on it until the veins swell, then grasp above the ankle joint with the company and the vein's position at the heel, outward towards the instep.
Many veins branch off from the man's face, so one should ligate the largest vein of them or at the ankle when it is gathered together; this is better and safer. If one has ligated it on the man's face, then he must take care not to damage the nerves that lie beneath the skin of the man's face.
Make the ligature by twisting as if you were to cut him off and let the surgeon be steady, if the bleeding does not cease, then make his foot wet with hot water and leave the blood to flow in it until it is exhausted, if the one ligating makes a mistake in the ligature on the first attempt.
Make the ligature above a little bit, for the place is safe and does not fear harm if you take care of the nerve as we said, and do the same with the saphenous vein on the other side of the man's leg, whether it be this or that. And as for the femoral vein, its place is as we said, at the groin on the wild side.
And the benefit of ligating it for a hip injury if that is due to hot blood, and how to ligate it: the patient should enter the bath and hurry and grasp his leg from the hip up to the ankle with four fingers wrapped in a thin long cloth; then it will only be apparent by doing so.
If it appears, then ligate it in whatever condition you find him, either by twisting or cutting off a piece or splitting it open; his place is safe, and this is so for most people; if you do not find it.
And if it does not appear at all, then ligate some of its branches, which are the ones that show on the back of the foot towards the heel and ankle, take care of the nerves and send out as much blood as you want.
Then remove the ligature and put a bandage on the site of the ligature, and tighten the place; for it will soon heal.
The Sixth Ninetieth Chapter.
In Hemostasis and the Method of Its Use.
The lancets may be of bronze, wood, iron and glass; the hemostasis is twofold: one type being with a constriction and the extraction of blood, and the other without a constriction.
And this hemostasis which is without a constriction is two-faced.
The lancets of the armpits, which are on both sides of the neck from all directions, and the lancets of the jugular vein, which is under the lower jawbone of the mouth, and the lancets of the shoulders, and the lancets of the hamstrings at the back of the thigh, and the lancets of the forearms, and the lancets of the shins.
The hemostasis is what draws the blood from the visible arteries that are embedded in the flesh; and for this reason, the strength does not fall like a phlebotomy nor should the hemostasis be used with fire or without fire in any of the diseases that are due to congestion until the body has been relieved.
If necessity demands hemostasis due to a disease or as a habit, we have used it at all times in the first, middle and last of the month and at any time.
And that is because some people, when blood accumulates in them to the extent that they need to be bled with hemostasis, find heaviness and headache in their head; and some of them find congestion and redness on their face, head, and neck; and some of them find itching and pimples on their face and navel, and darkness and drowsiness in their eyes; and some of them have numbness at the place of their hemostasis.
Some of them laugh a lot, and some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth, and their gums tremble and they spit out blood. Some of them sleep a lot, and some of them see in their sleep blood, redness, and cuts, and what is similar to that, so whenever we see something like that.
And particularly if it occurs in the middle of the month, we are ordered to perform cupping after two or three hours have passed from midday. As for the benefit of cupping on the neck, it benefits the weighty head and brings water to the eyes.
But it is necessary that this be done after the entire body has been purged and this cupping can serve as a substitute for purging bile. One should beware of using it if someone has cold-headed blood or has an attack, because it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold-headed illnesses should not use it, nor should someone who is addicted to it because it will cause forgetfulness in them.
Therefore, the cupper should be ordered to lower his hand slightly towards the bottom of the vein in fear of causing forgetfulness, and as for cupping on the calf, it is a substitute for purging the black bile and purging the pleura.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma and shortness of breath, wheezing, coughing, spitting up phlegm, and fullness. And one should lift the cupping on the calf slightly because if it is directed towards the bottom, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping on the ears, it benefits from headaches occurring in the head and face, neck, and throat, and toothache. It is a substitute for purging the pleura, and one should order the cupper not to deepen his hand in the fold so that he does not cut any veins and cause bleeding.
What concerns bloodletting under the tongue is effective for removing tumors in the mouth and decay of the gums, and similar diseases that occur in the mouth. It can also take the place of cupping on the cheeks. As for bloodletting on the shoulders, it is beneficial for hyperventilation caused by overeating and heat.
And as for bloodletting on the lower abdomen, it is beneficial from what benefits cupping on the three prominent veins, namely the basilic, cephalic, and saphenous, because it draws blood from all those hidden veins in the flesh and draws those hidden veins from thicker ones until it reaches the three thick veins.
It is necessary for the bloodletter not to pierce deeply in the area, because it is exposed flesh with nerves and veins underneath. As for a single cupping on the anus, it is beneficial for haemorrhoids and wounds in the lower part.
It is necessary for the bloodletter to make the cup large and from copper, because the area needs a strong suction and perhaps the glass cup will break and cause many punctures. As for cupping on the legs, it reduces swelling in a clear manner.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder, and relieves menstruation and is beneficial for boils and carbuncles, and takes the place of cupping on the sides and ankles except that it exhausts the body greatly and causes dizziness in most people. Cupping on the ankles is almost as beneficial as bloodletting on the legs.
The manner of placing the cups is that you first place an empty cup and then apply a moderate suction without prolonging its placement, but rather quickly applying and removing it in order to allow the pus to come forth evenly and not yet stopping until you see the area has turned red and opened.
Blood appeared, so then he tightens and redoes the bandage gradually, then looks at the state of the bodies, so if someone from among the people has soft flesh and is porous in pores, it is necessary that he tightens only one bandage, lest the place becomes disarranged.
The barber is ordered to widen the bandage and deepen it slightly, and to adjust the cloth gently and softly. If there is thick blood, he should bind twice: in the first instance, he should open a path for the flow of blood and its moisture; and in the second instance, he should strive to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is extremely bitter, he should rebind once more to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a little blood, one bandage suffices; but if we want to remove much blood, we bind with multiple bandages, and if it is thick blood.
It is necessary to bind a deep bandage and the moderate depth in the bandage only reaches the skin, as for what should be used from ointments when dressing wounds and water, and what the patient should avoid. However, if his skin is hard, rigid, and coarse, with narrow pores.
It is necessary to anoint the places of wounds with open, mild, and resolving ointments. However, if it is in the time of summer, then like anointing with sweet almond oil or violet oil or honeyed walnut oil or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in the winter, then like anointing with lavender oil or valerian oil or balm or hyacinth and so on. However, if the flesh is thick and cold, then let the ointment be mustard oil or camphor oil or panax oil or sassafras and so on.
If the patient has open pores and looseness of the flesh, it is necessary that he abstain from ointment. And these ones should wash their wounds after bloodletting with rose water or cold water or mulberry juice or pumpkin seed juice or grape juice and so on. And if his blood is very moist.
Washes his legs with kohl or water of the asab, samgh, and similar substances. If his swelling is severe, he washes his legs with old wine or water of murkhoosh, tharija, or bapungh, and it is necessary to beware of bloodletting in the bathhouse and after the bathhouse.
It is necessary that it be used after leaving the bathhouse for an hour or two, and it is not allowed for anyone to sleep after bloodletting. What is necessary is that the patient and the one who is having the bloodletting prepare themselves before the bloodletting and afterwards.
It is necessary to look first; if the patient or the one having bloodletting has a sanguine temperament and his blood is sharp and inflamed, then it is necessary for him to take cooling substances such as pomegranate, henbane with kohl, thyme, and scents like jasmine, and to make his food fatty foods, tender goat meat, and sweetmeats, and the like.
And if he has a cold temperament, it is necessary for him to drink honey wine and date juice or sweet scented skunk's horn, and to consume moderate perfumed wine that is between the old and the new. He should be ordered to eat little food and make his food fatty foods, green stalks, and white pigeon food, and it is necessary for the drink on the day of bloodletting and cupping to be more than the food.
And it is necessary for some people in certain times to drink the antitoxin, musk medicine, or Shilta before bloodletting and cupping, or after it, in order to strengthen the main organs and purify the blood, and it is not allowed for the abstainers.
And as for the bloodlettings that are unconditional, they are the ones that are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, belly, and back of the thigh because these organs do not tolerate it. Rather, its purpose is to attract the blood from one organ to another.
We place the compress on the breasts in case of a cough or we use it to remove cold air from the part. If it is placed on the abdomen and loins, then it relaxes the part, warms it up, and removes the pain by dispersing it.
And it is placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone, so that its power may be used to draw out the obstruction or dislodge the stone from its place. Similarly, it does this if placed on the liver and spleen when there is a chill in them. This compress can also be used empty with vinegar only.
And it is used with fire and also filled with lukewarm water in the case of swelling. This compress is filled with hot water alone or with water that has been boiled with some herbs suitable for that purpose.
Then it is placed filled on the location, held, removed, and repeated as needed. And this is an image of the compress that is used with fire:.
Its mouth is two fingers open as we have depicted, and its depth is half a finger's width. On the side of it, about half of that distance away, there is a small hole just large enough for a needle made from Chinese copper or yellow copper with thickened edges.
Smooth and flat, so that it does not hurt the part when placed on it. And in its center there is a recessed space made of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed. This compress can be made larger or smaller according to the diseases.
The manner of using this compress with fire on the part is that you take a wick from a tightly rolled cloth or a small candle and place it in the middle of the compress, which has been prepared as described.
The flames will ascend upwards towards the lower part of the wound so that the patient's body is not burned. Then, the dressing will be placed on the member and the finger will be placed on the hole we mentioned until we secure the dressing as needed, remove the finger.
The steam emerged from that hole and the dressing dissolved at its place. Then, the thread was pulled on the skin and returned to its original state if it was needed again. As for the dressing used in treating scrofula with water, there is no inserted rod or hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only, and this is its form:.
And this dressing, whenever it was large enough to hold a lot of water, was better.
The 97th Chapter.
In the treatment of dropsy.
The dropsy is used most of the time in the organs that cannot be wrapped with a dressing, either because they are small like the lip and palate and similar ones, or because the organ is exposed from flesh, like the finger and nose and similar ones.
And as for the manner of its use, take the dropsy that is in pure fresh water from the sources, then leave it a day and night in the fresh water until it is drained and there is nothing left inside. Then the body will be purged first by vomiting or bleeding, then the affected member will be cleaned.
Until it turns red, then a dressing will be placed on it. If the swelling subsides, it fell; and if possible, bandage the place with a dressing, otherwise wash the area with vinegar, then with much water, massage, and squeeze; and if bleeding continues after the dropsy has subsided, take a linen cloth wet in cold water.
And place it above until the hemorrhaging ceases; if the bleeding is heavy, sprinkle powdered alum or sycamore on it, or something similar from the astringent substances, until the bleeding stops or apply half a cup of bounded agaric to the area.
Leave it until the ligament sticks in place, then the blood stops, and what is needed is to reattach the ligament. Do not attach that ligament if another can be found. If the ligament refuses to stick, clean the site with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the site until something emerges from the blood, then put it down. If it feels something of the blood, it will stick to the place. And if you want it to come off, sprinkle some salt or sand on it, and it will fall onto the place.
The Third Chapter.
In Setting [or Bandaging].
This chapter also contains what is needed in the art of medicine, namely setting fractures and treating broken bones that occur in the bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by ignorant physicians and common people who have not consulted a book on the subject nor read a single line of it.
And because of this reason, this art of medicine has been non-existent in our country, and I have never seen a single good one in it. And I have only gained from it what I have gained through the length of my reading of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted the science from them, then I devoted myself to experimentation and apprenticeship throughout my life.
And I have described for you everything that is contained in my knowledge in this chapter. And I have applied my experience to it after having presented it to you and simplified it from the excessive length, shortened it as much as possible, clarified it as much as clarification can be, and illustrated for you in it many forms of the devices that are used in it.
As is the case with excessive explanation, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no strength except by Allah the Exalted, the Great.
The First Chapter.
In this chapter and sections of what pertains to the breaking of bones, it is necessary to present them and before we start mentioning each broken or disjointed limb separately, it is necessary to mention here at the beginning of this book general principles and chapters. You are compelled first to understand it and stand on the truth of it, for you alone among those who are eager to learn this noble craft are not you.
I say that whenever one of the bones is broken or dislocated or splintered or falls, it is necessary to hasten first to treat it or reduce it or all together. Unless prevented by an obstacle such as weakness of power or what has happened to him being a child or elderly, or the time being extremely hot or extremely cold indeed.
Then he should limit his food to cold legumes and bird meat and fish, and abstain from drinking and fatty meats and indulging in food, and every food that fills the veins with blood until if the swelling subsides and does not expect an accumulation of matter to the place.
Then the patient should return to his initial routine, and if the broken bone is in need of setting, it is necessary for the patient to eat nourishing foods that are abundant, thick, and tender, such as barley, rice, heads, and mutton, and cow's hooves. And eggs, cooked fish, and thick drink, and the like. For by this regimen, the setting of the fracture is faster and better, and know that broken bones in old men and elderly people cannot reunite and heal to their natural state at all.
The bones of the elderly are stiff and brittle, and may reunite and heal in some cases, just as the bones of young children do. But nature forms on the broken bone from all sides something like a kind of gruel that thickens and adheres to it until some parts stick together with others.
Some of them bind together until they reach the utmost strength and security as they were at first, so that nothing hinders their actions.
Therefore, it is necessary to provide the patient with nourishment that contains strength, moisture, and thickness as we said. And know that fractures differ in kind according to the difference in organs, for a fracture of the tibia is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the back.
And so are all the other organs, each one different from the others. And we will mention each type of fracture separately in its own chapter, and the type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
Because it may be a compression fracture without splinters, or it may be a longitudinal fracture with splinters and loose fragments, some of which are free and others not. And the fracture may occur with a wound and tear in the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
And each type has its own special trick in setting it, as will be mentioned separately in its place. And what is known about a bone fracture is its deformation and deviation from the normal position, and its appearance to the sense of touch and sensation when you touch it with your hand.
So when there is no apparent deformation, no sensation or feeling when you touch the bone with your hand, and the patient does not experience much pain, it is not a fracture, but rather a bruise, a slight crack or a minor break, and it should not be manipulated at all.
But rather, apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later when it suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if a bone is crushed and separated into two pieces without splinters, except that each piece has become detached from its owner.
You should begin by adjusting and straightening it before a hot abscess occurs to it, if a hot abscess occurs to it then leave it for some days until the hot abscess subsides, then smooth it as you are able with gentle touch and skill.
Know that setting and straightening it is easier than the bone in which fragments have occurred, then tie up what will be mentioned later. If the bone has fragments, then you must lengthen the broken limb on both sides by hand or foot.
Either by your hand if the limb is small, or by two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let your setting be such that you place the limb in its natural position until the broken bone extends, then return those fragments to their places as you are able with skill and gentle touch.
And strive to avoid causing pain or distress to the patient through your action. Strive to bring together one of the bones with its fellow in the best possible position, and at that time touch and feel them with your hand. If you see something contrary to nature, correct it as far as your ability permits.
And beware of excessive stretching and strong pressure, as many practitioners do, and often cause a hot abscess or inflammation in the limb, as I have observed them doing repeatedly.
Then bind the limb after setting, precision and stretching, so that it remains at rest and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it during his wakefulness, sleep, or when changing position, or during his urination and all his movements, until it is completely healed.
And that is because whenever the patient feels pain or discomfort in his usual position, he should transfer him to another position where he does not feel pain, and strive for that position to be straight and steady.
If the member is not splinted, then it will become distorted. As for the manner of splinting a broken member, I will describe it to you as I see fit. Know that broken members differ in their smallness and largeness and thickness, so what is smaller among them like the arm and finger and shin and similar parts.
It is necessary for the bandages of the splint to be soft and gentle, whereas what is rougher among them like the thigh and back and chest should be firm and rigid because the wide bandage is required for the large member and tightens it from all sides with equal pressure without any defect.
Then you start by applying the dressing corresponding to that. On the swollen part of the break then start wrapping the bandage on the same spot three or four turns according to what the member deserves, and hold tight with your hand on the bandage, then go up to the upper part of the break.
By holding it slightly less tightly than the break, then you move away from the break a little with the wrapping and loosen the hold a little bit until you take something from the correct position, then take another binding and wrap it also on the broken place.
Wraps, then go down with the wrapping to the lower part of the break. And be your action in tightening and loosening the wrap as we mentioned in the first upper wrap, then put between the wraps some swelling or bruise that matches the distortion of the break.
If there is distortion, then do not leave anything in it; then wrap another binding on top of it and smooth out these tight folds with your hand if there is no puffing or heat swelling in the member.
If there is puffing or heat swelling, then apply to it what calms that swelling and goes away with the puffing and leave it for a few days; then wrap tight on it when that time has passed. The wraps should be from coarse papyrus strips cut straight or should be wraps from willow wood splints.
That which is made from cypress wood or palm fronds or juniper or sycamore and similar things that are available, and let the size of each splint be as described in this shape exactly.
Except that the splint put on the fracture itself should be thicker and a little wider than the other splints, as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what is suitable for the broken member, large or small.
Then it is bound with another bandage to the splints, according to your first tightening exactly, and then it is secured from above with the tightened threads as we mentioned, that being that your tightening should be more on the site of the fracture. And every time you are away from the fracture, the tightening should be less.
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and firmness, and they should be made of wet cotton in particular, for if the threads are thick like what I have seen some ignorant people do, using unfolded cotton strips, which is a very great mistake.
Because the tightening with it goes beyond moderation, and the firm threads are also not suitable because you do not achieve what you want to tighten, and it is not right that there should be less than a finger's width between the splint and the other splints.
If the patient complains of the edges of the splints after being tightened in the correct places, then place under them a layer of flax or untangled wool until it no longer causes him any discomfort. And if the fracture is accompanied by an injury and there has been a tear in the skin, we will deal with that separately.
And know that not every broken bone should be tightened with splints from the first day, for if the member is large, it is not necessary to put splints on it except after five or seven or more days according to your judgment about the risk of developing inflammation.
This is the description of the dressings that the ancients used to apply to fractures, dislocations, and sprains. This is a general and simple dressing that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women because it does not cause heat or cold.
And it is that one takes from the dust of the millstone, which clings to its edges when the grinder is in motion. He kneads it as it is, without mixing it with egg white, and makes it neither thick nor thin, then uses it.
The description of the dressing that sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains. It is taken from ten drams each of opium, vetch, henbane, hellebore, and poppy, and five drams each of mercuric salt, myrrh, and Armenian or Roman clay. All of these are pounded and mixed with myrrh water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then this dressing is used; it is a good composition that sets broken bones quickly and is suitable for most people due to its moderation.
The description of another dressing that is also beneficial for fractures and sprains. It is taken from ten drams each of myrrh, opium, white henbane, mercuric salt five drams, vetch six drams, Armenian clay twenty drams. All of these are pounded finely and mixed with water or egg white and used.
The description of a dressing for dislocated joints and bones that have gone out of their places, to alleviate the pain that accompanies them, and to calm the shock caused by the fracture. It is taken from old wool that is soaked in vinegar and cooked oil, and placed on the spot; this dressing does not have the power to set bones.
But it is excellent in cooling the inflamed swelling and driving away pains, in particular. The description of another dressing that sets broken bones: one takes the bark of the persimmon tree and the prickly leaf, pounds all together, and applies them moistly. The description of another simple dressing used when a bone is freshly broken.
And you want to analyze the remainder of the tumor, take from each one a small part of the onion, celandine, and gentian root, all together, and mix them with indigo if the member is not inflamed; but if it is inflamed, mix them with water or vinegar and use it.
Formula for a poultice stronger than the first one, used when a hard tumor occurs in the bone, takes from the origin of onion, tamarind, and sage, as well as mallow, crown flower, and rue, each one part.
Mix all of them together and mix with vinegar or sweet water or indigo, all that according to the warmth of the member and its quiescence; but as for Galen, he did not mention in his book putting it on a broken bone when setting it.
Except for the binding agent made from beeswax and oil, without anything else; and Galen described it to be of medium consistency between thickness and softness. As for Galen, he thought that on setting a broken bone, one should use things containing gelatinous substance with something warm like myrrh, saffron, and resin, and so forth as mentioned.
Formula for a poultice that is beneficial for weakness and pain, takes from the following: safflower, gallnut, human hair or bird down, onion, and salt. All of these are crushed and sifted together and mixed with water, then applied as needed; and as for how long the bandage should be left on.
Then dissolve it, but only if you have observed that the pain and swelling have subsided, the bone has not shifted from its place, and there is no inflammation; otherwise, do not dissolve it for many days, lest severe inflammation or anxiety occurs.
Then dissolve it at the right time and do not delay; and remove the poultice from it. Then take a clean linen cloth or sponge, damp but not wet, and immerse it in warm water, and wash the area until the inflammation subsides and the pain subsides, then leave the member to rest for a moment.
Then wrap him with the polluted wool, soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, and tie it on him until the warm tumor subsides and the member's inflammation goes away and its pain departs. Then return him to gentle compression and slight binding, but do not bind him as tightly as your first binding.
And nurse him until he recovers, if you see that the tumor, redness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have gone away and you relied on compression and binding, then treat him as you did at first, whether or not anything happens to the member mentioned.
Do not remove the wrapping except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days, each according to what appears to you from the condition of the member as we said, until if the fracture becomes necessary and the flesh begins to form over it.
Also add at that time to the binding more than your first binding, and also increase the thickening of the patient's food as mentioned earlier. If you see that the site of the fracture has dried up and is not receiving what it should receive, know that the nourishment is being prevented from reaching it.
If you see that the patient has been wrapped in cold water at every wrapping, and this is done every three days, and reduce the binding slightly, for by doing so, nourishment reaches the member quickly and it recovers soon. As for what the foolish ones do to those who have broken their members once more.
For if it does not heal as it should and heals with a twist, that is the mistake of their actions and a great calamity, even though it may be correct as mentioned in their early books and practiced by them, and I have seen no one among them having any effect in that. And the truth is, do not practice it.
The Second Section.
On the fractures in the head.
Types of fractures in the head are many and varied, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures may occur due to a sword blow, and they can either split the bone entirely until it reaches the membrane that is beneath the bone. Like splitting wood.
And therefore, this type of fracture is called a "qudumi" and sometimes the sword may cut some of the bone and split its surface only without penetrating to the end, which is called a "complete excision". And the wound of these two fractures can be either great or small.
And there are fractures that are flaky or cracked and their cause is a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone, or something similar. This fracture can be either superficially reaching the membrane beneath the bone or it can be on the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures can also be either great or small. And there are fractures that are hidden in the bone, in the thick hair, which is a slight indentation.
And therefore, this type of fracture is called "sharri", and from it there can be a fracture that occurs due to a fall or a stone being dropped, and the like, which enters into the bone's surface and becomes hollowed out like copper that has been struck and has a part enter inside.
And that is more likely to happen in the moist skull of young men. And there can be fragments, both loose and not loose, among all these types of fractures, and we will discuss their treatment at its place, and we will recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And inspecting them with scales and removing the putrid flesh from them. And as for the type of fracture known as "sharri", it is recognized by exposing the bone, wiping and smearing it with a liquid, because the fracture appears black. And as for the treatment of the fracture, one looks first to the symptoms of the patient.
If you see in his symptoms what indicates a clear indication of fear, such as diarrhea, extension, loss of consciousness, cessation of voice, fainting, high fever, change in the eyes and redness, and similar symptoms, do not approach him nor treat him because death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then take him into treatment, and this is because if the wounded person comes to you at first when he was injured and it was during the winter days.
You should exert yourself in removing the bone before the fourteenth day, under all circumstances, and if it is during summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, so that what is beneath the bone does not decay and expose those symptoms which we have described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is accompanied by swelling and inflammation, then you should cut off the deformed part of the injured person's skull as I have described to you, which is that you should shave the head of the wounded person.
And you should expose the bone in whatever way it is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what lies beneath the patient, if an exposure reveals bleeding or warm swelling, then face that with what is necessary.
And this is that you should pack the place with a moistened cloth soaked in wine, rose water and vinegar, then grease the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding ceases, then take up the work of straightening the bone and removing it, which will be done by one of two methods.
One of these two methods is that you should cut the bone with a thin and narrow chisel, and this is its shape:.
Then you should use another chisel after this chisel, which is slightly wider than it, and this is its shape:.
Then you will also use another cutting, wider than the second and it is necessary that you have several cuttings different from each other, some being wider than others and some being shorter than others, and they should be in a great state of sharpness, made of Indian steel or good iron.
And use a hammer in hitting the cutting, lest the head becomes dislocated and injured; if the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to pierce around it before using the cuttings with the chisels which we called non-penetrating chisels.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the skull bone to what is behind it, in order that the chisel has a rounded corner on what is below its sharp head, resembling a belt or small circle that prevents it from piercing and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take many of these chisels, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone, until you are prepared for every skull with a chisel for the sharp edge on its length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and this is an image of three kinds. From large chisels to medium-sized ones to small ones:.
And as for the way of piercing around a broken bone, it is that you place the chisel on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has given way, then move the chisel to another position and make after each piercing a thickness of about the width of a reed or similar.
Then cut with the cuttings between each piercing and do that with great care as we said, until you loosen the bone either by hand or by something else from one of the instruments I prepared for that.
Like the saw and fine rasps, and it is necessary to beware all care that the chisel or cutting should touch anything from the membrane, for if you loosen the bone and remove it from the membrane, if it was attached to it and extracted, then you must cleanse.
Smoothen what remains of the bone with another tool that resembles a scraper, except that it should be finer and softer than all other scrapers. If there are any small fragments or shavings of bone remaining, take them gently with whatever tools you have.
Then treat the wound with lint and ointments that will be mentioned. As for the other side of the work, it is a very easy passage far from pitfalls, as Galen has said and praised greatly, and this is his statement. You should first start by removing a part of the bone from the location where it is more broken and prominent, until you have removed that part and exposed what lies underneath, as shown in this diagram:.
The flat part of it is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is on its sides in a direction parallel to the length as you see. It should stand up against the membrane, and the edge of the scraper is then struck from one side only.
With a small hammer until it cuts all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane, no frightening incident occurring there, even if the surgeon is inexperienced or busy, or falls asleep.
If there is anything remaining of the membrane attached to some parts of the bone, scrape it away from the bone with the same flat part of the scraper itself and release it gently, for it will release it without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane and what is broken off from the bone remains, with roughness and shavings remaining inside, you should remove that roughness and extract those shavings with a scraper made of different materials in various proportions, so that you can use it wherever needed and tailor it to the requirements of the work.
The broken bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you use in your case the thickest of these ligaments at first, then use afterwards one thinner than it and continue to do so until you reach the finest and most delicate ones.
And as for the other small hairline fractures and slight breaks, it is necessary that you treat each one according to what leads to its healing, which is something not hidden from anyone who has any experience in this craft and stands on what we wrote and demonstrated in large breaks.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, it is necessary that you take a piece of cotton cloth according to the size of the wound, soak it in rose water, and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another piece, triangular or trapezoidal. And soak it in wine and rose water, and place it on the first cloth, and do that with the least amount you can manage so as not to burden the integument, then use it above a broad bandage and don't tighten it except as much as it takes to hold the wound only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the swelling subsides, then release it and use afterwards the medicines that are with it, such as roots of sown thyme and fine coriander and Khandariq and Zarnaud and similar ones, and do not make them into pills.
And apply it to the wound as it is, and in general, use a medicine that can clean without inflaming, and exert your utmost effort when treating the wound to make it clean, not containing any fat or grime, nor allowing pus to accumulate there at all.
Because pus, if it accumulates on the membrane of the brain, corrupts and spoils it; and an affliction of great severity occurs to the patient because of that, and even more so if the skull is exposed when the bone is uncovered.
Swollen on its surface, look at it; if it is exposed to the manifestations we mentioned earlier, then know that it is doomed, and even if the swelling only occurred due to medication applied to it and was in the power of that medicine to do so.
You should take a part from honey and three parts from rose ointment, then mix them well together and spread the mixture on a cloth, then place it on the abscess and treat it with all the treatment methods necessary until it heals.
The third chapter.
Broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except its upper part entirely or one of them, because they are two prominent bones; the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but rather becomes deformed, bent, or crushed.
If one of its parts breaks, then you should insert your small finger into the nostril and gently push that fracture from inside with your index and middle fingers from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; do this carefully and check that your action does not cause harm or pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use a stick with a slightly thickened end to set it; if the fracture is on both sides, do the same thing; and begin to apply treatment on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, and if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided, then insert a thread of linen cloth into the nostril; if the fracture is in one side, use one thread, and if it is on both sides, use two threads.
Let the thread be as thick as it can fill the nostril, and some of the earlier splint makers said that you should grease the thread with fat and change it every day; but I do not see this being necessary, rather let the thread be soaked in white egg mixed with saffron powder.
Then leave the splint until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens, and sometimes in the nose's place where the splint is, put reeds from a goose after wrapping them with linen so that it prevents breaking of the nose lest the patient be unable to breathe.
And this is not a necessary thing if you wish to make it and if you wish to make the splints, for in case of nasal exposure during your work or boil with vinegar or cloth moistened with wine or something from the Deiankhilun ointment.
And if there is no boil, then it is necessary to apply a thin layer of sesame oil and calamus powder that we have mixed with egg whites, then place a linen strip on top without binding the nose in any way; if the bones of the nose are broken into small pieces.
Or if it breaks apart, then you must cut through it and bring it out with the instrument that is suitable for it, then sew the incision and treat it with whatever medication is appropriate for that; and if there is an injury inside the nose, then treat it with a splint and use lead pipes. until it heals.
The Fourth Chapter.
Repair of the lower jawbone if it breaks.
If the lower jawbone breaks and it is not accompanied by an injury, then if it breaks from outside only and does not break in two and crack open to the inside, then knowing it will facilitate treatment.
Then if the break is on the right side, you should insert your left thumb into the patient's mouth and if it is on the left side, you should insert your right thumb and lift up the bone fragment from inside to outside with care.
And your other hand from outside the bone should control it, so if the jawbone has broken into two pieces you should use a retractor on both sides to straighten it until it can be set, and if there is trauma to the teeth.
To separate what has united from it, and to make sure it stays attached with a thread of gold or silver or silk, then place the splint on the broken bone, then place a compress on it and put a large secure bandage or a piece of leather shoe-shaped as long as the bone. Then bind it from above as seems fitting to you, and adjust the wrapping until it is secure, so that it does not come undone. Order the patient to rest and calmness, and make his food a soft diet. If you think that something has changed in its shape in any direction, then attend to it on the third day.
Then bind it from above as seems fitting to you, and adjust the wrapping until it is secure, so that it does not come undone. Order the patient to rest and calmness, and make his food a soft diet. If you think that something has changed in its shape in any direction, then attend to it on the third day. Then smooth what you have applied with powder of frankincense, along with white of egg or fine breadcrumbs, after removing the splint from it. Place a poultice on the swelling, so long as that swelling clings to the bone and does not change its condition; then leave it be.
Do not remove it until the fracture has mended and strengthened. Often this fracture takes three weeks to strengthen, if a hot abscess appears during that time, then use what we mentioned repeatedly to subdue it until that abscess goes away.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then if there is a fragment or fragments of bone that have protruded, gently remove those fragments with what corresponds to your removal from the tool. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a file according to your need.
Then if you have removed those fragments and nothing remains, outline the mouth of the wound if it is wide, or else apply one of the remedies suitable for that. And close the wound until it heals.
The Fifth Chapter.
In Repairing a Fractured Clavicle.
The collarbone is more likely to fracture in front of the shoulder blade than at its end, and it usually breaks in one of three ways: either it fractures and separates into two parts without producing fragments; this is the easiest to repair.
And if the fracture produces fragments, which is more difficult to treat, or if it occurs with a wound, then the procedure would be as follows: if the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, have two attendants, one grasping the fractured vertebra and the other stretching the neck towards the opposite side.
Then you will shape the fracture with your fingers until it takes on its natural form and is free of notch or roughness. If needed, you should place a ball of linen or wool under the patient's armpit.
And the bone will be in the position you need, and then stretch and lift the vertebra and press the ball with your hand until the fracture takes on its natural form. If you are unable to pull one end of the vertebra outside due to it having sunk deep, then the patient should lie on their back.
And a pillow of medium thickness is placed under the patient's neckbone in the bone, and the attendant presses the neckbone down until the vertebra that was deep rises up above. Then the fracture is set and shaped with your fingers.
And if you feel that a fragment of the vertebra has cracked and is moving, then you should make an incision on it and carefully remove that fragment. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, then prepare one of the cutting instruments I have prepared for this purpose to cut it out. After that, place under the vertebra the instrument which holds back theomentum and this is an instrument made of wood or iron, as follows:.
