It resembles a spoon with no roughness, and its width should be according to the size of the bone that you need, whether large or small. As for its length, it should also be according to what is possible for you to do, and it should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower as you see.
If the incision wound that you made when removing the bone fragment is wide and you fear the hot abscess, then close the edges of the wound with sutures. If the incision is small or you fear the hot abscess, then deepen the wound by cutting and tearing it to the extent of the incision.
If a warm tumor appears, then break the papyrus in rosewater and vinegar or drink, and place it on it. And if the bone is tightened without injury or splitting, it is that you place on the bone the poultice made from wheat bran with white eggshell. And you put a splint on it, and then you put a ball under his arm if needed, then take a long bandage that is one or two fingers wide, then wrap a compress made from a thin board. It should be three fingers wide and in length as well, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the compress into the place on the bandage where the fracture is located, then tighten the bandage over the fracture like a spiral. And wrap it around his neck and under the sound arm and the affected arm, and wrap it several times on every side as you see fit, and how the tightening fixes the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And remember that the compress should not be removed from the fractured bone. And therefore the patient should be inspected every day, whenever the bandage has relaxed and you see that the compress has moved, then adjust it and tighten the bandage, then let the patient sleep on his back and put a small pillow under his arm at night.
So that his arm may rise with it and the fracture of the clavicle rises with the elevation of the shoulder or bind his arm to his neck, and do not remove the bandage unless a case occurs from a blister or swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the poultice if you see any signs of that. And wrap it tight and leave it until it heals and the fracture of the clavicle sets, and strengthens and grows stronger over twenty-eight days. It may be present in some people to a lesser extent.
The sixth chapter.
In repairing a collarbone fracture. It rarely happens that the collarbone breaks in its broad place, but rather it is the ends of it that break off; accordingly, whenever it does break in a certain spot or in the middle and this is known by touch, then according to what shape the fracture is in, form should be given to the repair.
To set it right and return it to its natural shape with all the skill you can, then apply to the place a poultice of wheat flour with egg white and linen thread and from above a broad plaster made of cloth.
Thin on the measure of the entire collarbone or slightly wider, if there is a swelling under the plaster in one of the places of the collarbone, then straighten that swelling with linen thread until the plaster lies flat, then pull from above with a long cloth.
Pull it tightly and secure the plaster so that it does not move from its place, and tighten the bandage every day; whenever the bandage relaxes, pull it tight again and adjust the plaster if it has moved from its place, and let the patient lie on their healthy side, and the collarbone will heal in twenty.
Or twenty-five days, and if this period has been completed, then release the bandage and you are safe; for it is one of the bones that do not fear breaking or collapse, but if a splinter of bone protrudes and was previously hidden under the skin, cut into it and remove it. And do what I mentioned in regard to a fractured wrist from reducing the inflamed swelling, if anything of that happens.
The seventh chapter.
In repairing a broken rib.
The chest may break in the middle and somewhat expose that, but its edges are more likely to break off and split apart; one of its symptoms if it breaks in the middle is that it will incline downwards and expose him to severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps the patient's blood pressure may increase and it becomes clear that the bone is fractured and not hidden from perception, and it is necessary for the patient to lie down on his back and a mattress is placed between his shoulders then his arms are bent and his ribs are brought together by his hands from both sides and he is laid flat as much as possible.
And wait until the shape of the bone returns as it should, then apply a dressing to it and wrap him with bandages and place a splint made from fir or sedge and similar materials on top of him in a sling after wrapping it in a cloth. Then wrap it tightly around the fractured bone so that it does not shift, and tie the bandage firmly onto his back several times, then check the bandage every time, and whenever it relaxes, tighten it.
And if necessity requires it, to remove it at the time of eating if there is exposure in that place or pain or swelling, then remove the dressing and treat what has appeared in a way that is suitable for its treatment, then apply the dressing again as needed. And so, and ensure it is tightened until he recovers.
The Eighth Chapter.
In the treatment of ribs if they are fractured, know that the fracture occurs in the hard areas below the back and its edges from the front, and it is exposed to view because they are dense and it is not hidden from perception when examined with the fingers.
And treat it by straightening the fracture with the fingers on the capable side until the shape becomes as it should, then dress and splint the broken bone if necessary. If the rib is fractured towards the inside, it exposes the patient to severe pain.
And it is like the bunch of thorns that exposes what has been done to those who have suffered from it, because the bone tears through the membrane and also exposes him to difficulty in breathing and coughing and profuse bleeding, and this is a difficult treatment, and the early physicians have employed many remedies in its treatment.
Some of them said that one should feed the patient what produces belching and flatulence so that the abdomen becomes inflated and expanded, and the fracture moves outside. And we dislike this lest it become a cause for the formation of a boil. If it did not happen, then if it has happened, it only increases in it and confirms it.
And some of them said that a compress should be applied to the place, then wrung out with force. It is similar to measuring, except that it is feared that the compress might draw moisture into the place due to its weakness. And some of them said that the place should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil.
And it becomes swollen in the area between the ribs until it is full, and the bandage is even then if it is wrapped around evenly. Then the patient is treated with treatment for spondylosis from food and medicine. If the patient is exhausted with something very severe that cannot be endured. And the bone presses against the membrane, pressing it in a deadly manner, and we fear for the patient. Therefore, we must incise the area, expose the broken rib, then lay beneath it the instrument which preserves the sac that was mentioned earlier. And we will cut the bone gently and extract it.
Then we will sew the edges of the wound if it is large, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If an abscess appears during that time, then we must be quick to apply a poultice of rose oil on the area and treat the patient with what calms the abscess from inside as well.
And he lies on the side that is most comfortable for him until he heals.
The Ninth Section.
In Repairing Fractures of the Back and Neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture occurs and it is rare that this happens and more common that it becomes inflamed, similarly with the vertebrae of the back. If this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not. If you look, and you see that his hands are shriveled up and numb, and he is unable to move them or straighten them or bend them, and if you try to flex them or relax them with a needle without him feeling it or experiencing pain in them, then know that in most cases he will not recover; he is lost.
And if he can move them and feels flexibility and relaxation, know that the spinal cord is intact and the patient will recover with treatment. If a back arrow (presumably a reference to a specific injury or condition) occurs in this manner, and you want to know whether it will also recover or not.
So look at his legs, if you see that they are shriveled up and have happened to them what has happened to the hands, then when he lies on his back the wind and stool come out without volition, and when he lies on his stomach the urine comes out without volition. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate but is unable to do so, know that he is lost and do not worry about treating him. But if none of this occurs, then the matter is less severe, and treatment for what has occurred is to apply cold compression to the boil.
By applying rose oil alone or with roasted egg whites to the affected area, apply it three times a day until the boil subsides. Then, place one of the strengthened and moistened dressings on the spot, tie it securely, and order him to remain still and quiet.
He should not sleep except on the side that does not cause him pain, until he recovers. If a bone fragment or something has broken off from the bone at the point of the boil, it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then, if the wound is large, you should stitch its edges together with thread. Then, treat it with ointments and creams until he recovers. If the last bone of the coccyx has been broken, it is necessary to insert the fingers of the left hand into the anus.
The broken bone is set with the other hand as much as possible, then the setting is stabilized and wrapped in cloth or bandage if needed.
If you find a shattered fragment in the wound, split it open and remove it, and treat the wound with treatment as mentioned until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
In Setting a Hip Fracture.
The vertebrae of the back do not easily break, but if they do, their breaking is that it becomes splintered at its edges and splits in length and tilts towards the inside and exposes itself to pain, numbness, and paralysis of the leg on its side.
And set it by passing your hand over it until you feel the shape of the break, and if the break is only in its edges, then set that break according to what presents itself to you from the setting until it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is in length or has tilted towards the inside, then prop the patient's body against his stomach until you can set that break. Then, after setting it, wrap it with a bandage and place a splint made of wood or leather over it, binding it tightly.
Do not fear for the break to move or the splint to fail, and set the unevenness of the wounds as much as it fills, until the binding takes on an even state. Order the patient to sleep on their back or healthy side, if a warm swelling presents itself.
Leave off applying the dressing and set him until the warm swelling subsides, then apply what soothes it according to what was mentioned before. Then return to setting him and bind him as is fitting, if something breaks off from his vertebrae or anything splits off from its edges; it should not be taken out.
And do not touch, but set him from the outside as we said, and leave his binding until he heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Fractures of the Upper Arm.
The upper arm is what lies between the shoulder and the top of the shoulder blade. If it breaks, treat it on one of two sides. One way is to take a straight, smooth stick of medium thickness in this shape:.
And bind it with two straps at its ends, then hang it from a high place and have the patient sit on a chair. Then, let them put their broken arm on the stick until their elbow is stuck in the middle of the bend of the stick, then hang something heavy above it.
Or have a servant hold it downwards, then the doctor will set the fracture with his hands together until the break returns to its proper place, and the other way is for the patient to lie on their back and have their hand tied to their neck with a strap.
Then command two servants to hold one above the break with their hands, and the other below it. Have each of them stretch in their direction. And if you want the traction to be stronger, tie a strap underneath the break and another on top, and have both servants pull in their directions.
If the break is near the shoulder blade, then it is necessary to have one strap under the armpit and the other under the break towards the elbow. And similarly if the break is near the elbow, then it is necessary to have a strap at that location and on the arm itself.
Then set the break gently without violence until the break is properly aligned and united with good consolidation, then splint it. If there is no inflammation, do not remove the splint for seven days, but apply a moistened cloth soaked in salt and rub with rose oil.
Until the inflammation subsides, then splint it. The method of splinting is to apply a compress on the break, then wrap a new cloth around the compress, and finally bring the arm together with itself and place the hand open on the shoulder blade.
Carry the stretch and tension on the shoulder and arm so that the arm stands in place of the splint, unless something prevents you from doing so or the broken bone changes its position. If it is likely to change its position, use the splint.
And it is that you place a splint on the break itself, which is wider and stronger than any other splints, and make three fingers' width between each splint, and the length of the splints according to the break, increasing by three fingers from all sides.
Then you tighten the tension on the splints as I mentioned earlier.
And it is that your tension should be stronger at the site of the break.
And whenever after the break the tension is less, if you see that the splints and tension are as we said when you immobilized the limb at the time of setting it, then do so.
And if you fear inflammation, leave the tension and splints alone. As we said, until the seventh day, then inspect the bandage every three days lest an abscess or swelling occur in the place, or nutrition be prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tension, so that everything is as we mentioned.
And if you are confident that nothing of that will occur, do not remove the bandage except after many days, and the patient should lie on their back with their hand on their stomach, and a padded splint made from wool placed under the shoulder, moderate in thickness.
And inspect at all times, day or night, lest the shape of the broken limb be distorted or the bandage loosen, and make sure that everything is as you said by making an effort, and make the patient's food gentle at first until the bone has strengthened.
And it is necessary to strengthen his food, for it is customary for the shoulder and leg to heal in forty days, so when that time has passed, you should remove the bandage and use a warm bath, treating with lotions that are suitable for that, unless the break was severe and not consolidated.
Do not loosen the bandage for fifty days or two months.
The Twelfth Chapter.
In the Repair of a Broken Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones and it is called the humeri, one being small, which is next to the shoulder, and the other being large, which is situated below the small one from underneath. Therefore, sometimes only the largest humerus may be broken or only the smallest one may be broken, or they may be broken together.
When the upper small humerus is broken, its repair is easier and recovery is faster. When the lower large humerus is broken, its fracture is more severe and recovery is more difficult. If both humeri are broken together, then if the broken one is the upper small humerus.
It is necessary for the doctor when repairing it to make the splint gentle and easy until he straightens it. If the large humerus is the one that is broken, then he should make the splint stronger. If both humeri are broken, then he should make the splint even stronger.
And it is necessary to shape the hand when repairing and stretch the splint on a pillow, with the shoulder of the hand above all the fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the index finger resting upon itself.
And let the pillow be beneath it in elevation so that the index finger does not experience difficulty.
Then a servant stretches the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another servant stretches it from above in like manner. Then the doctor straightens the bone until he puts it back to its best shape possible. If there are fragments of bone in the fracture, then he will reduce them.
Set each fragment in its place with effort. If a fragment appears to be exposed and is attached to the skin, and you have no hope of repairing it, then cut it off on the manner that we mentioned earlier. If there is a fracture with an open wound, then open up a pathway for him.
Treatment of that is taken from there, if an abscess appears in the first swelling and heat comes out; then smear a cloth with kohl mixed with henna and white beeswax, making it medium between thick and thin, and tie the cloth tightly until the swelling subsides.
If you remove the kohl, put the prepared dressing on the dusty road with white egg whites, then carry the bandages and make the one that is placed on the break slightly wider and stronger, and know that the number of bandages for the arm is six in most cases.
The break was in one bone or in two bones together, then make your bandage tighter and stronger on the location of the break, and whenever you turn it up or down, make the bandage slightly looser as mentioned earlier in the first chapter.
And make the cloth that wraps around the break a soft, wet linen, not too hard, and let the thread that binds it be made of special cotton, medium between thin and thick as we described, and inspect the limb and bandage after days.
If something needs to be corrected, like a swelling appears in the limb, then wrap the limb with warm water until that swelling subsides, and leave the limb unbound at night until it rests, then resume binding.
If the binding has relaxed and the bone is displaced or almost so, then correct all that effort, and also look, if the food cannot reach the limb due to excessive binding, then loosen it a little and leave it for days until food reaches it, then bind again.
If nothing from what we mentioned appears, then it should not be resolved until after twenty days or so, then attach the patient's hand to their neck, and let their arm be moderate, and preserve his effort from irregular movements and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two, and possibly in twenty-eight days, all depending on the temperament and strength.
The Thirteenth Chapter.
In Repairing Fractures of the Hand and Fingers.
Indeed, the palm of the hand and the tips of the fingers are rarely exposed to fractures, but rather they are frequently exposed to bruises, so when they are exposed to a fracture or bruise.
Then it is necessary for the patient to sit upright and in front of him a chair on level.
Then he places his hand on it stretched out, then the servant of the broken bones straightens them and the doctor sets them in good alignment. When this is done, it is necessary to apply a cast and padding if there is no hot swelling, then a splint is placed above as needed.
And I have wrapped it in a linen cloth. If the fracture is towards the bottom towards the palm of the hand, make a semi-spherical shape from the cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand then tie it with a long cloth.
And the cast should be made of skin, soft and pliable, so that it follows the curvature of the entire hand and is tied as needed. If the fracture is on the outside, it is necessary to make a cast above and another below in the hand, so that the hand remains open and standing.
Then the splint should be carried as the hand turns and is tied between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture is in one of the finger tips, if it is the thumb then set it straight as needed, and then tie it with the hand, and if you wish to make a small standing cast to support the fracture.
And it should not be moved. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, such as the middle or ring finger, or pinky, then set it straight and tie it to the adjacent healthy finger, or tie all of them together with a bandage, for this is better; or place a small standing cast on top.
As we said in the ambiguity and were puzzled when you approached and departed from the hot tumor, then you would meet it as it should be whenever anything of that kind occurred as previously described.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
On Setting Fractures of the Thigh.
The thigh bone is often broken and appears to the touch because it moves forward and backward, and its setting will be by binding a band above the break and another below it, with the patient lying on his face.
Then each band is stretched by an attendant in its direction, according to this balance, if the break is in the middle of the bone; and if it is near the hip, it is necessary to bind a soft band either with wool or something similar.
At the hip to towards the groin, so that the stretch is above and the other band below the break; similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the band should be near the knee, so that the stretch is downwards. Then the doctor will straighten the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape.
And the bone is straightened with a good union; then it is necessary to apply a dressing and compression, unless a hot tumor occurs in the member, in which case leave it for some days until the hot tumor subsides, then return to its treatment.
And as for compressing it, you should roll a thick and wide bandage around the break two or three times, leaving some of it over, then bend the leg until the ankle is at the origin of the fibula; and insert a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee from below and make the ends of the thread appear above on both sides.
Then turn the bandage around the leg and thigh as much as is left, then put poultices on the thigh in the same place as the break itself, and make one splint from it on the shin bone; and fill the gap between the thigh and leg with a soft cloth to level out the compression.
Then start by pulling the strong tendons from the middle to the break site three times or four, and each time you pull, make your pull weaker, softer, and gentler.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread that you had inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie on what follows from above. Then pass by the ends of the thread to the bottom until it reaches the ankle bone, and pull on both sides also.
So that the bandage does not come undone from its place, then leave the pull on it as long as the member does not become numb or swollen or inflamed or anything like that. If something like that happens, attend to it and correct what has happened in all of that as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a fragment of bone that protrudes, it is necessary to smooth it out if you can, and if not, split it open and remove it, and then treat the wound as we have mentioned earlier until it heals. This fracture may be pulled from the thigh without applying bandages to the leg.
As we mentioned in the arm and shoulder, except that this kind of splint does not cause a permanent disability if it is applied alone without including the leg, but the owner will always be lame.
And know that the thigh may take fifty days or a little more or less to heal, all of which is according to differences in temperament and other circumstances.
The fifteenth chapter.
In the fracture of the patella.
For the patella is rarely exposed to fracture and is often exposed to sprain, so if it does fracture, either it will split or break into its parts, and that may be with an injury or without.
And rely on all of that by sense, and splint it so that you smooth out what has separated into its parts with your fingers until it comes together and is reunited according to what you are able to smooth out and show compassion and skill. Then apply the dressing and place a splint on top if needed.
And it is pulled from above, corresponding to that; then it settles all the conditions we described in other fractures like hot tumors and so on, so that each opponent meets what is suitable for it until it heals.
The 16th chapter.
On setting the fracture of the leg.
Indeed, the leg has two bones: one thick and called the femur of the leg, and another thin and called the tibia, and it is exposed to various types of fractures that can occur in both arm bones. Therefore, setting it is like setting the arm bone, whether it is a single or double fracture; if both bones are broken together, the leg will turn in all directions, and if the thinner bone is broken, the leg will turn forward, but if the thicker bone is broken and this occurs from below, that is something that should not be hidden from you.
So use the traction and alignment, and bind the splints according to that, whether or not; but if it is a severe leg fracture with many fragments, then the traction should be less and more gentle, and you must take great care in setting it.
And in the leg, there is something additional to what is found in the arm, which is that when you have set the splints and finished all your work, take two spruce saplings of about the size that are used to level out the grooves between the planks or made from palm fronds or similar.
And choose one of them that has a slightly thicker diameter and is not from the reeds, let its length be equal to the length of the leg from the knee to the foot, then wrap around each one a strip of cloth wrapped twice along its length, and place one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it be from the knee to the foot, then tie the two saplings in three places on both sides and the middle; for with this binding, the leg will not incline to the right or left and will be firmly set, and a wooden splint can also be used along the length of the leg.
He should place it to preserve him from movement and as much as is necessary when the fracture has a special wound, then he must inspect the leg every day with great care about the swelling or inflammation or any other such thing. If anything of that happens, he should treat it as is necessary for it to heal, and the bone of the leg will be consolidated in thirty days or so.
The Seventeenth Chapter.
In fractures of the bones of the leg and fingers.
As for the ankle, it is not exposed to fracture at all, and as for the bones of the leg, they are sometimes exposed to fracture, and also the fingers, but very rarely, and in most cases, it is more susceptible to bruising.
If the leg bones are fractured and I have seen those bones come together, he should put the patient's foot on the ground standing upright like a mule, then you put your foot on what is raised of those bones and reduce it and straighten it.
until it returns to its place, then take the padding and gauze from above and put it under the sole of the foot as a small plate with two flat surfaces, then bind it tightly with the foot after wrapping it with cloth and all that is needed.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage, for you will find that the bones are set whether they were broken or not, and do as you see fit, but if some of the fingers have been fractured, then splint them and straighten them according to what I described to you in setting the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger, as long as the finger is and slightly thicker than it, then place this plate underneath the foot as I described to you and bind it tightly. If two or three or more bones have been fractured.
Then put a splint on each finger, as much as is needed, wrapped in a soft cloth and bind the plate to the sole of the foot, so that each end of the plate is outside the sole of the foot to hold it firmly.
You must not forget to treat all the fractures we mentioned, along with every injury that was mentioned, and respond to each condition as it requires.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
In the fracture of the woman's thigh bone and the shinbone of the man.
When the woman's pelvis is broken, place her on a stretcher then gently tilt her towards her back little by little and hold from behind, then fill the cleft in her pelvis with flax until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her pelvis.
Then the woman is shaken and her pelvis is lifted little by little, for that flax comes out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the pelvis and returns the bone fracture, then she puts a pad on her back; whenever she wants to urinate, she removes the flax gently until she does.
And then return it to its original state or so, and then go back to her first pad; she does that for seven days or thereabouts, because it heals. And if you want to use a catheter, tie a tube around her mouth and insert the entire catheter into her pelvis.
Then blow into the tube with force until the catheter inflates inside the pelvis, and the fracture is restored. Then fill it with flax and keep it as we described for several days until it heals. And if a man's or woman's shinbone is broken, use this method to treat it.
And treat it as we described for the hip bone, and it will not be hidden from you what is correct in these unusual fractures that rarely occur, especially those who have some experience and understand this book well, because the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
On the path of truth in treating it and setting it straight, understand this. And if a man's leg is broken at the thigh, with a swelling like an owl's wing, then insert the penis into it and place a cloth around it and bind it and leave it for three days or thereabouts until it heals.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
In setting fractures of bones when accompanied by a wound.
It is necessary for one who presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus or similar to it, that you begin to expose it as soon as possible with the conditions of exposure as we have mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding blood, then it is necessary that you begin to apply a compress of powdered white bean to it if something else is not available. Then take up the reduction of the fracture on that very day itself and do not delay.
If there is no inflammation, then do not touch the fracture. But if there is inflammation, leave the reduction until the ninth day until the inflammation subsides; do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, because it will be accompanied by bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is exposed and protruding on the skin, it is necessary that you try to reduce and align it with your hand gently and a little bit. And if it does not come under your hand to reduce and align it, use this instrument which is made of iron, its length being about seven or eight fingers. And apply it according to the size of the wound.
And therefore it is necessary for the doctor to take four or five of them according to what he needs in each type of fracture, and they should be rounded so that they will not bend when pressed upon during work.
And it should be sharp on one side, with a curve at the end of that side, and its top thicker, while its middle part much thinner, and this is its shape:.
And it is called by the Greeks Perim, which means a small clamp, so it should be made with its sharp edge curved to fit onto the protruding end of the bone and pushed forward until the bone returns and settles slightly; then use this clamp to align the ends of the fracture against each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not take hold of it well, then one should cut off that bone until the instrument can grasp it. If one cannot manage to reposition the bone as we have described, then shorten it according to its natural fissures which we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the rasps as best you can, then remove what remains on the bone from the roughness and fibrous scales. If after setting the bone you find that it is accompanied by a very severe and painful pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can set it to its natural position, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the injured person. After completing your setting of the bone, dip a compress in a strong black gum and especially if you are in summer; do not place anything on the wound that is greasy or oily.
So that putrefaction and corruption do not occur; then use the lint when you are finished with setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered, except to apply a layer of gauze on top of the folds as much as the size of the wound.
And beware with great caution not to suture the wound with the fracture, for many incompetent doctors have done so and caused their patients either death or gangrene or suppuration; and let your suturing be gentle and contrary to that of other fractures.
If the wound is old or large and some bad symptoms described by us appear on it, and he finds pain in the place which alarms him, then do not put lint on it; instead, make folds of hard linen in the place of the lint and suture him with it.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to discharge pus, then remove from it the linen you placed on it with the ointment; then use the poultice and lotions which are our customary treatment for wounds like quadruple ointment and others. And you should untie the bandage.
The wound should be inspected daily morning and evening until it heals and closes, and it is necessary to elevate the part not allowing pus to flow from it easily. If the wound has passed many days and the pus has not ceased flowing from it, know that there are small fragments of bone remaining.
It is necessary to inspect the wound with a probe, and if any of those fragments are exposed, remove them and take them out. And if none of them are exposed but are causing the part to be tight and producing pain, then cut them off and extract them in whatever way you can do that.
If an abscess or ulcer or another kind of corruption and foulness appears on the wound, then it is necessary to treat each one according to its nature with the treatment that is described in its place.
And what you should bear in mind and prepare yourself for, is if a large bone breaks and a piece of it falls onto the member like the femur or vertebrae or similar major organs, do not touch it or try to remove it. For many times death results from that, so leave it alone until it becomes detached, for perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place where treatment is possible, otherwise leave it alone.
The Twentieth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Complications that Appear after Some Fractures.
This complication often appears after the healing of a fracture, especially when it is near a joint, and it can deform the member and perhaps prevent the member from performing its natural function.
Look, if the complication is soft and swollen, use medicines that constrict like saponin, myrrh, and aloe, or similar substances by taking some of them or all of them and mixing them with a constricting drink or egg white or vinegar.
She will wrap it in a cloth, and stretch it well and tie it tightly. Then she should leave the binding for many days before untying it again. She should repeat this process until the swelling goes away if Allah wills it, or apply a lead sheet to it firmly.
If the lead has a special property that removes all congestion in the organs, then if the swelling becomes hardened and intensified and necessity urges its removal, cut it from above and remove the fatty tissue that forms or divide it with some forceps until it disappears and treat the wound until it heals.
The Twenty-First Section.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it has healed and the member remains soft and pliable after that on account of its original nature.
If a bone fracture has healed and the member afterwards remains soft and weak, it is due to many reasons. One of the reasons is either excessive loosening of the bandages and binding it in a way that is not proper, or excessively tightening the bandages until nutrition cannot reach the member, or excessive manipulation, or excessive movements at the wrong time, or lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
And the treatment for that is to nourish the patient and strengthen his body until blood circulation increases in it, and to use warm baths, and to bring joy and happiness upon him, and so on. Then apply linseed oil to the member to attract it to receive much nutrition or to continue its manipulation with lukewarm water. until food flows into it and returns to its natural shape.
The Twentieth Section.
In the treatment of broken bones that have healed but are distorted and prevented from functioning as they should.
So when a member that has healed shows distortion or bending of the broken bone or swelling and is prevented from taking its natural shape. Unless the member has refused to perform its natural function, it is not proper that someone should claim to break the member from its head. And many of the ignorant doctors and operators have done so in our country.
And this deed is greatly reprehensible, leading to a great and unbearable harm. However, if the twist and complexity are acute, it should be treated with hot water in which the herbs like mullein leaves, its root, and king's crown, and similar things have been steeped.
And it should be treated with poultices of the steeped herbs, like tightly-made diachylon or taking the mullein root and striking it with chicken fat and cypress tree resin, then applying it. Or, taking the sweet date and pounding it with dove's droppings and similar medicines which are called "breakers" of inflammation.
And the complexity should be treated with continuous gentle massage using one's hands, and moving the member in all directions at all times. If the deformation has developed and worsened, and stiffness and pain have occurred, and necessity requires treating it with iron.
So it is necessary to make an incision above and release the connection of the bone, cut off what remains of the complexity or bone with fine cuts, and use gentle care in doing so. Then, treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
The Twenty-Third Section.
The jaw is the displacement of a joint from its place, preventing movement and making the member ugly. It causes great pain and suffering to the sufferer. Therefore, whenever someone experiences jaw displacement, it is necessary that he act promptly to correct it.
He should not delay in the slightest, for if he delays, the place will become inflamed and it will be harder to correct the jaw. Therefore, he should not delay or procrastinate, but rather act promptly when it becomes inflamed, because many times it causes intense suffering and painful convulsions.
However, if that appears, it is necessary to advance to evacuate the abscess then leave until the tumor subsides slightly then wrap the member with hot water and fat then return gently and treat each member as mentioned in its place.
And I have arranged the chapters of the jaw according to what has been mentioned earlier in the fracture, from top to bottom of the body.
The 24th chapter.
In the treatment of the lower jawbone.
The maxillae rarely dislocate except in rare cases, and when they are removed, it will be on one of two faces; either they will slightly displace from their place and relax a little, or they will completely dislocate until they relax. Until it flows into the chest so that the patient's saliva drips and he is unable to hold onto it nor can he articulate his words by speaking, for if the dislocation is slight, it often returns of its own accord with minimal effort.
And if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its return with speed and not delay in the least, while having a servant hold the patient's head and the doctor inserting his single finger into the root of the jaw inside the mouth if the jaw is on one side.
Or he inserts all his fingers if the jaw is on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside manipulate it and orders the patient to open his mouth and let go so that it can move in each direction and the doctor manipulates the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return and especially if both jawbones are involved, one should use the poultice with hot water and fat until it becomes easy to return, and not delay its return at all as we have said. If they return and settle and the patient's mouth fits together without relaxation, then apply to them a poultice of linen cloth that has been made from wax and fat and oil.
Then he binds him next to a slack band, and the patient's sleep will be on his back with his head slightly raised between two pillows so that nothing moves him to the right or left nor does he chew anything but makes his food easy.
Until the pain goes away and the jaw is set, he should eat what he wants, and use that bandage loosely without tensing up when eating or drinking or yawning until the jaw is set and relaxed.
If the jaw becomes difficult to dislodge when it is stuck at some point and does not return to its place, many problems often occur from that, such as headaches and perpetual dizziness, or perhaps the patient's belly will run and they may vomit repeatedly, so if you see this, know that he is damaged.
And many people often die from that condition within ten days.
The 25th chapter.
In the setting of the jawbone and the corner of the neck.
As for the trapezius, it does not dislocate from the internal side due to its connection with the chest and can dislocate outwardly, and that will be evident by touch; and binding it requires the patient to lie on his back and stretch out his arms.
Then you should press the place with your hand firmly until it returns, then put on a compress and bandage and tie it, as for its side that is connected to the neck, it does not dislocate except in rare cases; if it were to dislocate one day, you should set it back and adjust it according to what we have mentioned and as seems best to you.
Then you should put on a compress and bandage and tie it, and order the patient to maintain gentle movement and calmness until he is relaxed, and with this very treatment, you can set right the corner of the neck if it has also strayed from its place.
The Sixth and Twentieth Chapter.
In the breaking of the neck vertebra.
Know that the neck vertebra breaks in three ways: one is that it breaks towards the armpit downwards, and the second is that it breaks towards the chest; and perhaps it may break above the neck vertebra, which is rare.
It does not break towards the back at the shoulder joint nor does it break forward at the point of the nerve, and more often it breaks and comes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have a thin build because it comes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
And as for those who have a thick build, it is the opposite, namely that it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty; and perhaps some people may suffer a blow or fall so that the neck vertebra becomes swollen and warm, and one may think it has broken, therefore one should investigate that until its soundness is confirmed.
Then one proceeds with its treatment, and identifies the break if it is towards the armpit downwards; if there is a gap between the broken neck vertebra and the healthy one, you will find a clear difference between them; you will also find that the head of the neck vertebra is damaged and under the armpit, when touched, the head of the neck vertebra is like an egg.
And the patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear or move it in all movements, and similarly if it has broken towards the chest or above, you will find that evident upon touch, not hidden.
And this break can be eased if it is fresh or the patient is young, and one should have a servant lift his hand up then place your fingers of your hand under his armpit and lift the joint with force upwards to its place and the servant should lift his hand and extend it upwards.
Then he should wrap it around the bottom, for it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the fracture has existed for several days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use the poultice that relaxes and softens like cooking the tops of cloves, hyssop, and royal crown in water and use it.
Then the patient lies on his back and places a wool ball under his armpit, moderate between relaxation and tension. Then the doctor places his leg on the ball and takes hold of the patient's hand with his own and stretches it down.
And pushes the ball with his leg, for it returns to its place. If you wish, he can be returned in this way: by having a taller man assist from the side and entering under the patient's armpit and lifting their arm up until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down to their belly. If the patient is light, it is necessary to attach something else to hold them up, for then the fracture returns to its original state, and sometimes it also returns in another way.
And that is by concentrating on a long wooden beam with a rounded head like a plowshare, neither too thick nor too thin. Then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a linen cloth over the top of the beam and the patient stands along its length.
Then he stretches his hand down to the other side and also stretches his body on that side with force, for then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it by all that has been mentioned, use this treatment: take a beam about two arm lengths in length. And make it four finger widths wide and two finger widths thick, with a rounded head to make it easy to enter the depth of the armpit in this shape.
Then he binds on the rounded head of the splint, a length of linen so that it will not cause harm to the injured limb. Then he places it under the armpit and stretches out his entire hand and arm down onto the splint, and binds the splint to the shoulder, elbow, and side of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a bar. The shoulder is raised and the hand is stretched down, leaving the rest of the body suspended from the other side, for the joint enters from its socket. If it can be done in any way, then a moderate-sized ball made of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared poultice is carried from the mortar with gum and egg white, all over the shoulder, as it turns above. Then the ball is pulled tight under the armpit tightly, and his hand is tied to his neck with a bandage.
And he is left not to move his hand for seven days, and it is necessary that the injured person's sustenance be a little limited until the limb becomes strong, as it is easier to heal. Then after seven or five days, he will be exercised by movement, and if it holds up and does not stiffen, then he has recovered.
If the joint keeps on dislocating many times due to moisture or another cause, then it is necessary to use three Sufafids as mentioned in the book of Sufafids. If this is done and the bandage is released after seven days but the joint does not hold.
The poultice was reapplied and tightened on him many times, but it did not hold and fell off, and he became limp and could not be lifted up again. Then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed or stretched out or become limp. Then know at that moment that the joint will never stay in its place again.
What is to be cut that is towards the chest and breast and behind it, is done by pushing and applying with the hand until it returns, using all of the pulling and treatment until it heals. If an obstacle appears in the member and slows down its movement.
Let the healer apply the poultice repeatedly many times until that member becomes soft and returns to its natural state.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In the treatment of the arm fracture.
The arm joint becomes dislocated quickly and also returns quickly, and it dislocates in all directions, especially in front or behind, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is under the sight and under the touch to any shape of dislocation.
And if you compare the dislocated arm with the correct one, it will be clear to you by a visible explanation of the joint's position and it cannot bend its shoulder or lean on it, and it is necessary that you act quickly and return the dislocation to its place before a hot inflammation appears.
And if a hot inflammation appears, it is difficult to return it and may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is behind, for then it becomes worse than any kind of dislocation and more severe and painful, and many times death follows with it.
And if it is possible to return it, have a servant stretch out both his hands and arm, with the doctor's hand above the arm and below it, and he pushes the joint with all his fingers or with the tip of his thumb until it returns to its place.
And as for if the dislocation is in front, it can return by bending one's hand once until it hits the tip of the thumb that is adjacent to it. If the dislocation does not return, use strong forceful pressure, which is to have servants stretch out the arm.
The surgeon takes assistants also, lest it be lost at the wrist. Then the arm is straightened in every direction after it has been wrapped with a cloth or a broad bandage. If the doctor begins to manipulate the joint, he should anoint his hands with ointment so that it will be easy to dislocate the joint.
Then the joint is pushed with a strong push until it returns. And after its return, it should be dressed with a bandage that contains grip and drying with egg white, tightened firmly, and the arm should be tied to the surgeon's neck and left for some days.
Then it is undone. If the joint remains in its place, then loosen the bandage from it and leave it alone. And if you see that the joint has not become stable, redo the dressing and bandage and leave it alone for some days until it becomes stable, then undo it. If a swelling occurs after the joint's return and release.
And if the movement is difficult, let him use moistening in the bath and gentle rubbing and stroking until it becomes soft or put a fat goat's tail on the joint then tie it and leave it there for a day and night then remove it and enter the bath. If sweat occurs, make the joint sweat moderately.
Then apply it to him three times, with bath, until it becomes soft. And if you wish to put boiled cow's fat on him, greasy and smeared with fat, do that several times for he will become soft and return to its original state.
The 28th Chapter.
Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist of the hand is often dislocated and its reduction is facilitated except for other joints. However, it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the moment it dislocates before the place becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms.
Set the affected arm on a board and have a servant support it. The doctor should place his hand on the joint and press until it returns, except that he must look to see if the fracture has gone into the depth of the hand.
Let the patient place his outer hand on the board during extension and flexion. If the fracture protrudes to the surface of the hand, let him put his inner hand on the board so that the doctor's hand is on the same joint. Then he should be able to return it in time; otherwise, bind it with a compressive dressing for swelling.
Leave it alone, for it cannot be restored and will not tolerate being returned after some days have passed; except that the joint will remain in its dislocated state and the patient will suffer nothing, unless the hand becomes inflamed and is unable to grasp anything.
Then you will learn that the nerve has been cut or crushed, and there is no remedy for it except to bind with a ligature; perhaps it will be beneficial, and perhaps it will not. If the arm is returned, then wrap it with the dressing we described, and bind and leave it for five days.
Then loosen it and exercise the hand. If its movement is stubborn and there is something rigid in it, soak it with warm water and massage several times until it becomes supple.
The 29th Chapter.
In the treatment of finger fractures.
The fingers may dislocate in every direction. When a finger dislocates to the surface of the palm or its back, extend the finger and press the dislocation with your thumb until it returns, then bind the tip of the finger and fasten it towards the direction it dislocated to.
Leave it for two days then release it and extend it until it becomes straight by day. If during the night you bind it as described, do not loosen it during the day but exercise it with movement and bind it at night; do that for several days until it hardens.
And you will do the same thing with it if it detaches to the inside of the hand, tying it to the same direction and doing with it what you did first until it is healed, and you will do the same thing with it whenever it detaches to any other directions.
The thirtieth chapter.
In the treatment of a broken vertebra.
When a vertebra or the neck vertebra is broken, or it is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are separated from their places, then there is no treatment for it because death will quickly reach the sufferer.
And a sign of that is that the sufferer's stool comes out without his will and he cannot retain it, and often some of his members become paralyzed, either his legs or his arms or one of them, and if one vertebra becomes detached from its place then many do.
And its dislocation will be to four directions, and the one that is detached to the back is called a haddah, and as for its treatment, it is that you should look at it; if the haddah has occurred from old age then there is no treatment for it whatsoever, but if it occurred from a fall or a blow or something like that.
I mentioned them previously with a lengthy treatment by which most of it does not lead to benefit, and I summarized what was needed from the majority in my approach, explained it, and corrected the device contrary to what they showed and explained.
I say that the haddah which occurs in front of the chest has no cure and cannot be healed, and likewise those on both sides also, but only the one which occurs in the back is treated as I described it, and that is by having the sufferer lie down on his face.
On a brick bed that is level and close to the wall, spread a wet cloth underneath and put a pillow for him so his chest does not get hurt, then place a wooden beam standing upright in a hole in the ground at the head end of the bed, and another beam at the foot end.
And a servant holds the board, and it should not be secure in the pit, and another servant holds the other on that form then wraps it around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft and tight bandage and stretches the end of the bandage to the board that is at his head and ties him in.
Then it wraps with another bandage above his shins, and above his knees, and at his ankles then gathers all the bandages together and ties them in the other board that is at his feet then each servant stretches to their side of the board with the bandage and the two boards do not move from their fixed positions.
Unless they are not secure as we said, and the doctor puts his hands on the splint with force until it returns or puts a board on it then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return by this treatment, he takes a board that is about three arm-lengths long.
And he digs in the wall which we said should be near the patient's place, and enters the end of the board into it then puts the middle of the board on the notch and the doctor's feet are at the other end and tightens it tightly until the splint is compressed.
And it returns to its place, and if you want to make it with the screw which is twisted by hand, he inserts it into the ground near the patient's head at the end of the trench, two wooden boards being a forearm long, and also at his feet at the end of the trench.
And it is so that after every two boards, a span of space is left, and in each board there is a hole made for the screw to go through, and all the wood is secured in the ground firmly so that it does not move at all and enters an iron rod slowly which is the screw that tightens the bandage in the holes of the two boards.
On its side, a hole has been drilled where a stick of a span long is inserted which bends and in the other two pieces like that. Then the bandages are tightened on the patient's chest in the loop that is at his head and the bandages that were tightened on his legs in the loop that is at his feet.
Then he stands at each loop, a servant unfolding his hand with the instrument that bends the loop, and the doctor adjusts the notch as we have mentioned, and this is the picture of the loop, the splint, and the patient.
Then, after the swelling subsides and the position is evened out, it is necessary to place a dressing of dried lint on top of the bandage, then the poultice, and put a splint from a flat piece that is three fingers wide or so, and its length should be as long as takes up the notch's position.
And on some of the correct beads, then it is bound with a bandage that needs to be used, and the patient should eat soft food until they recover. If there remains some swelling in the position at the end of the recovery period, it is necessary to use medicines that soften and loosen with the use of the splint we described for a long time.
And sometimes a lead sheet is used for that, and if there remains some swelling at the end of the back's vertebrae, it may be thought to be a dislocation and this can be an excessive bone that has protruded, so do not approach it with this treatment, as death may result.
The 30th Chapter.
In the Treatment of a Paralyzed Thigh.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the loin are only exposed to the flexion, but they are not exposed to what other joints are exposed to, such as slight rotation and sinking, and the joint of the hip is bent on four sides.
Therefore, it is dissected to the inside and outside and forward and backward, and more so to the inside and less so to the front or back. And the sign of its dissection to the inside is that if you join the healthy limb with the injured one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be bent more than the healthy one, and the injured person cannot flex their leg when walking; and the position that follows walking will become clear to us before the top of the thigh has reached there.
And the sign that exposes it to outside is contrary to these symptoms; and the sign that exposes it to forward is that he straightens his leg to its full extent, unless there is pain in the knee, but if he intends to walk, he will not be able to do so forward.
And he holds his breath and trembles his thigh, and when walking, his step falls on the heel; and the sign that exposes it to back is that he does not straighten the knee nor can he flex it before flexing the thigh, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
And the thigh is relaxed, and the top of the femur is at the level of the pubic bone clear; and as for the return of this wound, it is that you look: if the wound is old and has become chronic with its owner and has not been attempted to be reversed and has remained in its state, then there is no cure whatsoever.
So it is not suitable to expose it; and as for the one whose wound is new, regardless of what type it was from the four sides of the wound, then hasten to manipulate the joint, stretch it inside and outside, and move it left and right, so that maybe.
He returns and does not need any other treatment; if he does not return like this, then it is necessary to prepare a servant with a strong arm, one to stretch his leg from the bottom either by his hands or by a rope that binds above the knee, and another servant to support him from above.
That the operator should enter his hand from underneath his armpit, then tie a thin cord around the origin of the thigh and grasp the end of the cord with a servant's hand, the extent of which may be either in front from the direction of the scapula to the direction of the popliteal fossa or else behind towards the direction of the back.
And all of them should be in one extent until the patient raises his body from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of extent is common to the four types; if the jaw returns as we mentioned or else, it will be necessary for each type that I have mentioned to have its own specific treatment.
However, if the jaw returns to its original position, then the patient should lie on his correct side and a cord should be placed around the origin of the thigh between the head of the femur and the location underneath the scapula.
Then the bandage should be extended upwards from the direction of the scapula to the top of the body towards the popliteal fossa, and another servant should grasp with his strong arms and hold the thick part of the upper thigh and extend it outside with great force, for it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than all the other types of treatment that involve returning this member to its place; if it is difficult for you and does not allow you to enter into this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to bind the patient's legs altogether with a strong cord, thin on the ankles and knees.
And it will be after each of them, the amount of four fingers, and the upper leg will be extended more than the other one by two fingers, then the patient will be suspended on a beam in the house, being two arms' length above the ground.
Then order a strong servant to grasp the head of the femur and another servant to suspend the patient, and push with all his might the servant who is grasping the femur, for the joint returns to its place quickly; as for returning it to its original position if the jaw is outside.
The patient should be laid down on the operating table as we described in "The Owner of the Wound". The bandage should be tightened on his injured leg, especially, and on his chest. Then two sticks are placed one at his feet and the other at his head. An additional stick is placed in the middle of the operating table firmly.
A moist cloth is wrapped around it so that the patient does not get hurt. So that the stick is between his thighs, lest he slide down when he leans forward. Then each servant approaches his side and the doctor adjusts the ankle with his hands, if possible.
Unless that, a board is placed on him and it is secured as we mentioned in "The Owner of the Wound", regardless. However, it is necessary for the patient to be laid down on his healthy side, and if the wound is towards the front, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this position on the operating table.
And the doctor places his right hand on the upper edge of the wound, then presses it with the other hand, yet keeps the pressure extended downward to the knee level. If the wound is at the back, it is not necessary for the patient to lean down.
And he is raised above the ground but it is necessary for him to be placed on something solid, just as his foot should be bent outward according to what we mentioned from his position on the operating table and he is facing down with the bandages tightened.
As we mentioned earlier, it is necessary to use the clamp on the board also at the location where the joint has come out. If the ankle joint has returned to its proper position as it should be and there is no sign of its return, which is that the patient's leg extends.
If you see them staying in place and the patient is flexing his leg without any difficulty, then know that the joint has returned to its proper position. At that time, bind the thighs together and apply the bandage with a knot that does not allow the ankle to move in any direction.
It is necessary for the surgeon. The rest is three or four days, then loosen the bandage and poultice and split the leg with another one, if you see them equal in size, know that the break has healed, so release the patient to walk, and if you see something of relaxation.
Go back and reattach it, and tie it as you did at first, and leave it also for three days, then loosen it. And gradually increase her walking on it until she is well, if Allah wills.
The second and thirtieth sections.
In the treatment of a fractured knee.
The knee fracture is on three sides, it fractures inward and outward and downward, meaning backward, but does not fracture forward at all. And the sign of its fracture is that the surgeon should bind his leg to his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. And if all its fracture faces are united, then have the surgeon sit down with his legs outstretched as much as he can manage and have a servant sit behind him to hold his middle and tie it slightly back, then you sit on his thighs.
And lean your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee. Then tie his knee with force on both sides with your hands, and another servant will extend his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg sticks to the thigh in a smooth, natural way without being forced. Then bind it and stick the leg to the thigh, then tie them altogether with three or four bandages. Then release them, and he should not use anything except for light walking until she gets strong.
If it is difficult for you to make her return as we described, and otherwise use the strong stretching with bandages that I mentioned in the treatment of a fracture until she returns.
The Chapter Thirty-Third.
In the treatment of a broken ankle.
The ankle may become dislocated by a slight degree or entirely, and the dislocation may be either inwardly or outwardly. A sign of dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
And as for the treatment of a slight dislocation, it is easy to reduce and involves gently stretching his arms out and aligning him until he returns to normal. And if it has become entirely dislocated, then the patient should sit down and be held by a strong servant or attendant from behind his back in the middle.
Then grasp his right hand with your right hand from above and his left foot from below at the ankle joint, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, and then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the shin without force.
You should do that twice as we described, then push the front of the foot towards the shin in the third time while pulling on the ankle joint. If it returns to its normal shape in one or two attempts and you see the foot is straight, but if not, then repeat the process until it does return.
And if it does not return as we described, then have the patient lie on his back on the ground and grasp his thighs securely to prevent him from falling between them, having wrapped a cloth around them so that he is not hurt, then the attendant should hold his thigh.
Then another attendant should hold the patient's leg, either by his hands or with a strap tied around his ankle, then each attendant should pull in the opposite direction of the other, and the splint should be held between the patient's thighs to prevent his body from moving downwards during the process, then the physician should straighten the joint with his hands.
And the servant grasps the healthy leg down below, then the jawbone returns quickly. If the jawbone returns and you see its correctness, then carry the poultice and gauze and bind it with strong ties, and secure the foot with a tie down below. One must beware of the nerve that is above the ankle from behind.
So that the tie is not too tight and hurts him, then leave it for two or three days. If the swelling subsides, bind it again and then release it on the third or fourth day. And the patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he walks before this period, it is not guaranteed that the jawbone will not be damaged and spoiled, nor will it accept treatment after that. If a hot abscess appears, one should use what we described earlier to treat it in any other place of the wound until it passes.
The 34th Chapter.
Treatment of the Finger Bones of the Hand.
It is necessary to straighten what has become bent with a slight extension without force, which is not difficult but easy. If the finger bone is in some of the joints of the foot, then it is necessary for the patient to place his foot on a level surface from the ground.
Or on a board, and he is standing like a horse. Then get up and put your foot in what remains of those joints, and then press it with your foot with strength until it returns and you see that it has straightened and does not show any defect in the place.
Then put a board under the arch of his foot, and it will take all the toes, making two rows. Then bind it tightly and securely for three days, then release it and keep him away from walking for many days until it strengthens and is guaranteed to return if God wills.
The 49th Chapter.
Types of jawbone that occur with a wound or fracture or both.
When something of that sort occurs and the swelling is treated and sutured, it often results in death.
Therefore, it is not permissible to attempt treatment of that sort unless one is experienced in the craft, having spent many years learning and practicing with patience and compassion, not being reckless or hasty, and initially using medicines that calm hot swellings only, and then allowing the patient to recover.
Praise be to God except for what I have hoped for in the patient's recovery from the injury, with a slight improvement in their condition and some hope evident in their face, so that you may cure them at the first opportunity before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to its normal condition, use the treatment that calms hot swellings and treat the wound with ointments suitable for it. If the jawbone is fractured and there are detached fragments of bone, remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned regarding simple diseases and their treatment in their places.
And strive with all your might and cleanse yourself of entering the path of evil as we have instructed you, for that will be to your benefit and salvation if God wills.
